\section*{FOREWORD}

We take great pleasure in presenting this book of mathematics to the students of polytechnic colleges. This book is prepared in accordance with the new syllabus under ' M " scheme framed by the Directorate of Technical Education, Chennai.

This book has been prepared keeping in mind, the aptitude and attitude of the students and modern method of education. The lucid manner in which the concepts are explained, make the teaching and learning process more easy and effective. Each chapter in this book is prepared with strenuous efforts to present the principles of the subject in the most easy to understand and the most easy to workout manner.

Each chapter is presented with an introduction, definitions, theorems, explanation, solved examples and exercises given are for better understanding of concepts and in the exercises, problems have been given in view of enough practice for mastering the concept.

We hope that this book serve the purpose keeping in mind the changing needs of the society to make it lively and vibrating. The language used is very clear and simple which is up to the level of comprehension of students.

We extend our deep sense of gratitude to Thiru. R. Sornakumar Coordinator and Principal, Dr. Dharmambal Government Polytechnic College for women, Chennai and to Thiru P.L. \subsection*{1.1 DETERMINANTS}

\section*{Definition:}

Determinant is a square arrangement of numbers (real or complex) within two vertical lines.
Example: $\left|\begin{array}{ll}a_{1} & b_{1} \\ a_{2} & b_{2}\end{array}\right|$

\section*{Order:}
$A=\left|\begin{array}{ll}a_{1} & b_{1} \\ a_{2} & b_{2}\end{array}\right|$, consisting of two rows and two columns is called a determinant of second order. The value of the determinant is $\Delta=a_{1} b_{2}-a_{2} b_{1}$.

Example: Let $A=\left|\begin{array}{cc}2 & -5 \\ 1 & 3\end{array}\right|$
$|\mathrm{A}|=(2)(3)-(1)(-5)$
$|A|=6+5$
i.e $\Delta=11$

Determinant of third order.
The expression $\left|\begin{array}{lll}a_{1} & b_{1} & c_{1} \\ a_{2} & b_{2} & c_{2} \\ a_{3} & b_{3} & c_{3}\end{array}\right|$ consisting of three rows and three columns is called a determinant of third order.

The value of the determinant is obtained by expanding the determinant through any row or column with proper sign attached starting from $\mathrm{a}_{1}$, the first row first column elements, we will have positive and negative sign alternately.

Example: $\left|\begin{array}{ccc}+ & - & + \\ 1 & -1 & 3 \\ - & + & - \\ 0 & 4 & 2 \\ ++ & - & + \\ 11 & 5 & -3\end{array}\right|$ expand thorugh first row.
$=+1\left|\begin{array}{cc}4 & 2 \\ 5 & -3\end{array}\right|-(-1)\left|\begin{array}{cc}0 & 2 \\ 11 & -3\end{array}\right|+3\left|\begin{array}{cc}0 & 4 \\ 11 & 5\end{array}\right|$
$=1(-12-10)+1(0-22)+3(0-44)$
$=-22-22-132$
$=-176$

\section*{Minor of an element:}

Minor of an element is a determinant obtained by deleting the row and column in which that element occurs.

Example:
$\left|\begin{array}{ccc}1 & -1 & 3 \\ 0 & 4 & 2 \\ 11 & 5 & -3\end{array}\right|$
Minor of $-1=\left|\begin{array}{cc}0 & 2 \\ 11 & -3\end{array}\right|=0-22=-22$
Minor of $0=\left|\begin{array}{cc}-1 & 3 \\ 5 & -3\end{array}\right|=3-15=-12$

\section*{Co-factor of an element:}

Minor of an element with proper sign attached is called co-factor of that element.
Example:
$\left|\begin{array}{ccc}+ & - & + \\ 3 & -2 & 1 \\ 2 & 0 & -3 \\ 4 & 5 & 11\end{array}\right|$
$$\begin{aligned}
\mathrm{Co}-\text { factor of }-2 & =-\left|\begin{array}{ll}
2 & -3 \\
4 & 11
\end{array}\right|=-[(22)+12] \\
& =-34
\end{aligned}$$
$$\begin{aligned}
\text { Co-factor of } 0 & =+\left|\begin{array}{cc}
3 & 1 \\
4 & 11
\end{array}\right| \\
& =+(33-4) \\
& =+29
\end{aligned}$$

Note: The sign for the element $\mathrm{a}_{\mathrm{ij}}$ is $(-1)^{\mathrm{i}+\mathrm{j}}$

\section*{Properties of determinant:}

\section*{Property (1):}

The value of the determinant is unaltered by changing rows into columns and vice versa.
$$\text { (i.e) }\left|\begin{array}{lll}
a_{1} & b_{1} & c_{1} \\
a_{2} & b_{2} & c_{2} \\
a_{3} & b_{3} & c_{3}
\end{array}\right|=\left|\begin{array}{lll}
a_{1} & a_{2} & a_{3} \\
b_{1} & b_{2} & b_{3} \\
c_{1} & c_{2} & c_{3}
\end{array}\right|$$

\section*{Property (2):}

If any two rows or columns of a determinant are interchanged then the value of the determinant is changed in its sign only.
$$\text { (i.e) }\left|\begin{array}{lll}
a_{1} & b_{1} & c_{1} \\
a_{2} & b_{2} & c_{2} \\
a_{3} & b_{3} & c_{3}
\end{array}\right|=-\left|\begin{array}{lll}
a_{2} & b_{2} & c_{2} \\
a_{1} & b_{1} & c_{1} \\
a_{3} & b_{3} & c_{3}
\end{array}\right|$$

By notation $\mathrm{R}_{1} \leftrightarrow \mathrm{R}_{2}$
[Generally the rows and columns are denoted by $\mathrm{R}_{1}, \mathrm{R}_{2} \ldots \ldots$. and $\mathrm{C}_{1}, \mathrm{C}_{2} \ldots \ldots$. respectively]

\section*{Property (3):}

If any two rows or columns of a determinant are identical or same, then the value of the determinant is zero.
$$\text { (i.e) }\left|\begin{array}{lll}
a_{1} & b_{1} & c_{1} \\
a_{1} & b_{1} & c_{1} \\
a_{3} & b_{3} & c_{3}
\end{array}\right|=0 \quad R_{1} \equiv R_{2}$$

\section*{Property (4):}

If each element of a row or column of a determinant is multiplied by any constant $k \neq 0$, then the value of the determinant is multiplied by same constant k .

\section*{Property (5):}

If each element of a row or column is expressed as the sum of two elements then the determinant can be expressed as the sum of two determinant of the same order.
$$\begin{aligned}
& \text { i.e. }\left|\begin{array}{ccc}
a_{1}+d_{1} & b_{1}+d_{2} & c_{1}+d_{3} \\
a_{2} & b_{2} & c_{2} \\
a_{3} & b_{3} & c_{3}
\end{array}\right| \\
& =\left|\begin{array}{lll}
a_{1} & b_{1} & c_{1} \\
a_{2} & b_{2} & c_{2} \\
a_{3} & b_{3} & c_{3}
\end{array}\right|+\left|\begin{array}{lll}
d_{1} & d_{2} & d_{3} \\
a_{2} & b_{2} & c_{2} \\
a_{3} & b_{3} & c_{3}
\end{array}\right|
\end{aligned}$$

\section*{Property (6):}

Any determinant is unaltered when each elements of any row or column is added by the equimultiples of any parallel row or column.
$$\begin{aligned}
& \text { i.e. }\left|\begin{array}{ccc}
a_{1}+k a_{2} & b_{1}+k b_{2} & c_{1}+k c_{2} \\
a_{2} & b_{2} & c_{2} \\
a_{3} & b_{3} & c_{3}
\end{array}\right| \\
& \left.=\left|\begin{array}{ccc}
a_{1} & b_{1} & c_{1} \\
a_{2} & b_{2} & c_{2} \\
a_{3} & b_{3} & c_{3}
\end{array}\right|+\begin{array}{ccc}
k a_{2} & k b_{2} & k c_{2} \\
a_{2} & b_{2} & c_{2} \\
a_{1} & b_{1} & c_{1} \\
a_{2} & b_{2} & c_{2} \\
a_{3} & b_{3} & c_{3}
\end{array} \right\rvert\,+k(0)
\end{aligned}$$

\section*{Property (7):}

In a given determinant if two rows or column are identical for $a=b$, then $(a-b)$ is a factor of the determinant.
$$\begin{aligned}
& \text { i.e }\left|\begin{array}{ccc}
1 & 1 & 1 \\
a & b & c \\
a^{2} & b^{2} & c^{2}
\end{array}\right| \text { for } a=b \\
& =\left|\begin{array}{ccc}
1 & 1 & 1 \\
b & b & c \\
b^{2} & b^{2} & c^{2}
\end{array}\right| \\
& =0 \quad c_{1} \equiv c_{2}
\end{aligned}$$
$\therefore(\mathrm{a}-\mathrm{b})$ is a factor.

\section*{Solution of simultaneous equations using Cramer's rule:}

Consider the linear equations
$$\begin{aligned}
& \mathrm{a}_{1} x+\mathrm{b}_{1} \mathrm{y}=\mathrm{c}_{1} \\
& \mathrm{a}_{2} x+\mathrm{b}_{2} \mathrm{y}=\mathrm{c}_{2} \\
& \text { let } \Delta=\left|\begin{array}{ll}
\mathrm{a}_{1} & \mathrm{~b}_{1} \\
\mathrm{a}_{2} & \mathrm{~b}_{2}
\end{array}\right| ; \Delta x=\left|\begin{array}{ll}
\mathrm{c}_{1} & \mathrm{~b}_{1} \\
\mathrm{c}_{2} & \mathrm{~b}_{2}
\end{array}\right| \text { and } \\
& \Delta \mathrm{y}=\left|\begin{array}{ll}
\mathrm{a}_{1} & \mathrm{c}_{1} \\
\mathrm{a}_{2} & \mathrm{c}_{2}
\end{array}\right|, \text { then } x=\frac{\Delta x}{\Delta} ; \mathrm{y}=\frac{\Delta y}{\Delta}
\end{aligned}$$

Provided $\Delta \neq 0$.
$x, y$ are unique solutions of the given equations. This method of solving the line equations is called Cramer's rule.

Similarly for a set of three simultaneous linear equations in $x, y$ and $z$.
$\mathrm{a}_{1} \mathrm{x}+\mathrm{b}_{1} \mathrm{y}+\mathrm{c}_{1} \mathrm{z}=\mathrm{d}_{1}$
$\mathrm{a}_{2} x+\mathrm{b}_{2} \mathrm{y}+\mathrm{c}_{2} \mathrm{z}=\mathrm{d}_{2}$
$\mathrm{a}_{3} x+\mathrm{b}_{3} y+\mathrm{c}_{3} \mathrm{z}=\mathrm{d}_{3}$, the solution of the system of equations by Cramer's rule is given by
$\mathrm{x}=\frac{\Delta \mathrm{x}}{\Delta} ; \mathrm{y}=\frac{\Delta \mathrm{y}}{\Delta} ;$ and $\mathrm{z}=\frac{\Delta \mathrm{z}}{\Delta}$
provided $\Delta \neq 0$. Where $\Delta, \Delta x, \Delta \mathrm{y}$ and $\Delta \mathrm{z}$ are the determinents formed in the same way as defined above.

\section*{WORKED EXAMPLES PART - A}
1. Solve $\left|\begin{array}{ll}x & 4 \\ 9 & x\end{array}\right|=0$.

\section*{Solution:}
$\left|\begin{array}{ll}x & 4 \\ 9 & x\end{array}\right|=0$ By expanding we have
$x^{2}-36=0$
i.e $x^{2}=36$
$\therefore x= \pm 6$
2. $\quad$ Solve $\left|\begin{array}{cc}x & 2 \\ & 3\end{array}\right|=0$

\section*{Solution:}
$\left|\begin{array}{cc}x & 2 \\ x & 3 x\end{array}\right|=0$
expand $3 x^{2}-2 x=0$
$x(3 x-2)=0$
$x=0 ; \quad 3 x-2=0 \quad 3 x=2$
$x=0$ or $x=\frac{2}{3}$
3. Find the co-factor of 3 in the determinant $\left|\begin{array}{ccc}1 & 2 & 0 \\ -1 & 3 & 4 \\ \text { Solution: } & 6 & 7\end{array}\right|$.
$$\begin{aligned}
\text { Cofactor of } 3 & =\mathrm{A}_{22}=(-1)^{2+2}\left|\begin{array}{ll}
1 & 0 \\
5 & 7
\end{array}\right| \\
& =(-1)^{4}[7-0] \\
& =+7
\end{aligned}$$
4. Write down the minor 3 in the determinant $\left|\begin{array}{ccc}1 & -2 & -1 \\ 3 & 4 & -4 \\ 5 & 0 & 2\end{array}\right|=0$.

\section*{Solution:}

The minor of $3=\left|\begin{array}{cc}-2 & -1 \\ 0 & 2\end{array}\right|=-4-0$

\section*{PART - B}
1. Find the value of ' $m$ ' when $\left|\begin{array}{ccc}m & 2 & 1 \\ 3 & 4 & 2 \\ -7 & 3 & 0\end{array}\right|=0$.
Solution:

Given $\left|\begin{array}{ccc}m & 2 & 1 \\ 3 & 4 & 2 \\ -7 & 3 & 0\end{array}\right|=0$
expand along $\mathrm{R}_{1}$, we have
$\mathrm{m}(0-6)-2(0+14)+1(9+28)=0$
$-6 m-28+37=0$
$-6 m+9=0$
$-6 m=-9$
$6 \mathrm{~m}=9$
$\mathrm{m}=\frac{9}{6}=\frac{3}{2}$
2. Using Cramer's rule solve $2 x-3 y=5 ; x-4 y=8$.

\section*{Solution:}
$2 x-3 y=5$
$x-4 y=8$.
where $\Delta=\left|\begin{array}{ll}2 & -3 \\ 1 & -4\end{array}\right|$
$$=-8+3=-5$$
$\Delta x=\left|\begin{array}{ll}5 & -3 \\ 8 & -4\end{array}\right|=-20+24=4$
$\Delta y=\left|\begin{array}{ll}2 & 5 \\ 1 & 8\end{array}\right|=16-5=11$
By Cramer's rule
$$x=\frac{\Delta x}{\Delta}=\frac{4}{-5} ; \mathrm{y}=\frac{\Delta \mathrm{y}}{\Delta}=\frac{11}{-5}=\frac{-11}{5}$$
3. Find the values of ' $x$ ' when $\left|\begin{array}{ccc}x & 6 & 3 \\ 1 & x & 1 \\ -2 & 4 & x\end{array}\right|=0$.
Solution:
$\left|\begin{array}{ccc}x & 6 & 3 \\ 1 & x & 1 \\ -2 & 4 & x\end{array}\right|=0$
$x\left(x^{2}-4\right)-6(x+2)+3(4+2 x)=0$
$x^{3}-4 x-6 x-12+12+6 x=0$
$x^{3}-4 x=0$
$x\left(x^{2}-4\right)=0$
$x=0, \quad x^{2}-4=0 \quad x^{2}=4$
$x=0, \quad x= \pm 2$

\section*{PART -C}
1. Using Cramer's rule. Solve the following simultaneous equations,
$x+y+z=2$
$2 x-y-2 z=-1$
$x-2 y-z=1$

\section*{Solution:}
$$\text { Let } \begin{aligned}
\Delta & =\left|\begin{array}{ccc}
1 & 1 & 1 \\
2 & -1 & -2 \\
1 & -2 & -1
\end{array}\right|=1(1-4)-1(-2+2)+1(-4+1) \\
& =1(-3)-1(0)+1(-3) \\
& =-3-3 \\
\Delta & =-6 \neq 0
\end{aligned}$$
$$\begin{aligned}
\Delta x & =\left|\begin{array}{ccc}
2 & 1 & 1 \\
-1 & -1 & -2 \\
1 & -2 & -1
\end{array}\right|=2(1-4)-1(1+2)+1(2+1) \\
& =2(-3)-1(3)+1(3) \\
& =-6-3+3 \\
\Delta x & =-6 \\
\Delta y & =\left|\begin{array}{ccc}
1 & 2 & 1 \\
2 & -1 & -2 \\
1 & 1 & -1
\end{array}\right|=1(1+2)-2(-2+2)+1(2+1) \\
& =1(3)-2(0)+1(3) \\
& =3+3 \\
\Delta y & =6 \\
\Delta \mathrm{z} & =\left|\begin{array}{lll}
1 & 1 & 2 \\
2 & -1 & -1 \\
1 & -2 & 1
\end{array}\right|=1(-1-2)-1(2+1)+2(-4+1) \\
& =1(-3)-1(3)+2(-3) \\
& =-3-3-6 \\
\Delta z & =-12 \\
x= & \frac{\Delta x}{\Delta}=\frac{-6}{-6}=1 \\
y & =\frac{\Delta y}{\Delta}=\frac{6}{-6}=-1 ; \quad \mathrm{z}=\frac{\Delta z}{\Delta}=\frac{-12}{-6}=2
\end{aligned}$$

\subsection*{1.2 MATRICES}

\section*{Introduction:}

The term matrix was first introduced by a French mathematician CAYLEY in the year 1857. The theory of matrices is one of the powerful tools of mathematics not only in the field of higher mathematics but also in other branches such as applied sciences, nuclear physics, statistics, economics and eletrical circuits.

\section*{Definition:}

A matrix is a rectangular array of numbers arranged in delete rows and columns enclosed by brackets.

\section*{Example:}
1) $\mathrm{A}=\left(\begin{array}{ll}1 & 2 \\ 3 & 4\end{array}\right)$;
2) $\mathrm{B}=\left(\begin{array}{ccc}2 & 1 & 0 \\ -5 & 6 & 7 \\ 1 & 0 & 8\end{array}\right)$

\section*{Order of a matrix:}

If there are ' m ' rows and ' n ' columns in a matrix, then the order of the matrix is $\mathrm{m} \times \mathrm{n}$ (read as m by n ).

Example: $A=\left(\begin{array}{lll}a_{1} & a_{2} & a_{3} \\ b_{1} & b_{2} & b_{3}\end{array}\right)$
The above matrix A has two rows and three columns. We say that A is a matrix of order $2 \times 3$.

\section*{Types of matrices:}

\section*{(1) Row matrix:}

A matrix having only one row and any number of columns is called a row matrix.
Example: $\mathrm{A}=\left(\begin{array}{lll}1 & 2 & -3\end{array}\right)$

\section*{(2) Column matrix:}

A matrix having only one column and any number of rows is called a column matrix.
(3) Square matrix. $\left.\begin{array}{r}\text { Example: } \mathrm{B} \\ \text { (3 } \\ 5\end{array}\right)$

A matrix which has equal number of rows and columns is called a square matrix.
Example: $\mathrm{C}=\left(\begin{array}{ccc}2 & -1 & 0 \\ 3 & 4 & -2 \\ 1 & 5 & 6\end{array}\right)$ is a square matrix of order 3 .

\section*{(4) Null matrix (or) Zero matrix:}

If all the elements of a matrix are zero, the matrix is called zero or null matrix.
Example: $O=\left(\begin{array}{lll}0 & 0 & 0 \\ 0 & 0 & 0 \\ 0 & 0 & 0\end{array}\right)$

\section*{(5) Diagonal matrix:}

A square matrix with all the elements equal to zero except those in the leading diagonal is called a diagonal matrix.

Example: $\mathrm{D}=\left(\begin{array}{ccc}-1 & 0 & 0 \\ 0 & 3 & 0 \\ 0 & 0 & 2\end{array}\right)$

\section*{(6) Unit matrix:}

Unit matrix is a square matrix in which the diagonal elements are all ones and all the other elements are zeros.

Example: $\mathrm{I}=\left(\begin{array}{lll}1 & 0 & 0 \\ 0 & 1 & 0 \\ 0 & 0 & 1\end{array}\right)$

\section*{(7) Determinant of a matrix:}

Let ' A ' be a square matrix. The determinant formed by the elements of A is said to be the determinant of matrix A. This is denoted by $|\mathrm{A}|$.

\section*{(8) Singular and Non-Singular matrix:}

A square matrix ' $A$ ' is called a singular matrix if $|A|=0$. If $|A| \neq 0$, then the matrix $A$ is called a non-singular matrix.

\section*{(9) Transpose of a matrix:}

If the rows and columns of a matrix are interchanged, then the resultant matrix is called the transpose of the given matrix. It is denoted by $\mathrm{A}^{\mathrm{T}}$.

\section*{Algebra of Matrices:}

\section*{(1) Addition and Subtraction of matrices:}

If A and B are any two matrices of the same order, then their sum or difference i.e $\mathrm{A}+\mathrm{B}$ or $\mathrm{A}-\mathrm{B}$ is of the same order, and is obtained by adding or subtracting the corresponding elements of A and B .

\section*{Example:}
$$\begin{aligned}
\text { If } A & =\left(\begin{array}{cc}
4 & 3 \\
-1 & 0
\end{array}\right) ; B=\left(\begin{array}{cc}
2 & 1 \\
-3 & -4
\end{array}\right) \text { then } \\
A+B & =\left(\begin{array}{cc}
4+2 & 3+1 \\
-1-3 & 0-4
\end{array}\right)=\left(\begin{array}{cc}
6 & 4 \\
-4 & -4
\end{array}\right) \text { and } \\
A-B & =A+(-B) \quad-B=\left(\begin{array}{cc}
-2 & -1 \\
3 & 4
\end{array}\right) \\
& =\left(\begin{array}{cc}
4-2 & 3-1 \\
-1+3 & 0+4
\end{array}\right)=\left(\begin{array}{cc}
2 & 2 \\
2 & 4
\end{array}\right)
\end{aligned}$$

\section*{(2) Multiplication of matrices:}

Two matrices A and B are conformable for multiplication if and only if the number of columns in A is equal to the number of rows in $B$.

Note : If A is of order $\mathrm{m} \times \mathrm{n}$ and B is of order $\mathrm{n} \times \mathrm{p}$ matrices, then AB exists and is of order $\mathrm{m} \times \mathrm{p}$.

\section*{Example:}
$$\begin{aligned}
& \text { If } A=\left(\begin{array}{lll}
1 & 2 & 3 \\
3 & 0 & 2
\end{array}\right) \text { and } B=\left(\begin{array}{cc}
-1 & 2 \\
3 & 4 \\
4 & -3
\end{array}\right) \text { then } \\
& \mathrm{AB}=\binom{\begin{array}{|ccc|}
\hline 1 & 2 & 3 \\
\hline & 0 & 2
\end{array}}{\hline}\left(\begin{array}{|c|c}
\begin{array}{|c}
-1 \\
3 \\
4 \\
\hline
\end{array} \\
4 \\
-3
\end{array}\right)
\end{aligned}$$
$$\begin{aligned}
& =\left(\begin{array}{cc}
-1+6+12 & 2+8-9 \\
-3+0+8 & 6+0-6
\end{array}\right) \\
& A B=\left(\begin{array}{cc}
17 & 1 \\
5 & 0
\end{array}\right)
\end{aligned}$$

Generally $\mathrm{AB} \neq \mathrm{BA}$

\section*{Co-factor matrix:}

In a matrix, if all the elements are replaced by the corresponding co-factors of the elements, then the matrix obtained is called the co-factor matrix.

Example: If $A=\left(\begin{array}{cc}1 & -4 \\ 8 & 3\end{array}\right)$
Co-factor of 1 is +3 Co-factor of -4 is -8
Co-factor of 8 is $4 \quad$ Co-factor of 3 is 1
$\therefore$ Co-factor matrix is $\left(\begin{array}{cc}3 & -8 \\ 4 & 1\end{array}\right)$

\section*{Adjoint (or) Adjugate matrix:}

The transpose of the co-factor matrix is called the adjoint matrix or adjugate matrix. It is denoted by Adj.A

Exmaple: From the above matrix $A=\left(\begin{array}{cc}1 & -4 \\ 8 & 3\end{array}\right)$ and the co-factor matrix of $A$ is $\left(\begin{array}{cc}3 & -8 \\ 4 & 1\end{array}\right)$, the adjoint of $A$ is $\left(\begin{array}{cc}3 & 4 \\ -8 & 1\end{array}\right)$.

\section*{Inverse of a matrix:}

Let $A$ be a non-singular square matrix. If there exists a square matrix $B$, such that $A B=B A=I$, where $I$ is the unit matrix of the same order as that of $A$, then $B$ is called the inverse of $A$ and it is denoted by $\mathrm{A}^{-1}$. This can be determined by using the formula.
$$\mathrm{A}^{-1}=\frac{1}{|\mathrm{~A}|}(\operatorname{adj} \mathrm{A})$$

\section*{Note:}
1. If $|\mathrm{A}|=0$, then there is no inverse for the matrix A .
2. $\mathrm{A}^{-1} \mathrm{~A}=\mathrm{AA}^{-1}=\mathrm{I}$
3. $(\mathrm{AB})^{-1}=\mathrm{B}^{-1} \mathrm{~A}^{-1}$
4. $\left(A^{T}\right)^{-1}=\left(A^{-1}\right)^{T}$

\section*{Rank of a matrix:}

Let ' $A$ ' be any $\mathrm{m} \times \mathrm{n}$ matrix. It has square sub-matrices of different orders. The order of the largest square submtrix of A whose determinant has a non-zero value is known as the rank of the matrix A.

Generally the rank is denoted by $\rho(A), \rho(A)$ is always minimum of $m$ and $n$.

\section*{Note:}

The rank of a matrix is said to be $r$ if
1) It has atleast one non zero minor of order $r$.
2) Every minor of A of order higher than $r$ is zero.

\section*{Example:}
1. Find the rank of the matrix $\left|\begin{array}{ccc}1 & 5 & 6 \\ 2 & 3 & 4 \\ -1 & 2 & 2\end{array}\right|$.
Solution:

Let $\mathrm{A}=\left|\begin{array}{ccc}1 & 5 & 6 \\ 2 & 3 & 4 \\ -1 & 2 & 2\end{array}\right|$
$$\begin{aligned}
|\mathrm{A}| & =1(6-8)-5(4+4)+6(4+3) \\
& =1(-2)-5(8)+6(7) \\
& =-2-40+42
\end{aligned}$$
$|\mathrm{A}|=0$
consider the determinant of the second order
$\left|\begin{array}{ll}1 & 5 \\ 2 & 3\end{array}\right|=-7 \neq 0$
Hence the rank of A is 2 .
i.e $\rho(\mathrm{A})=2$

\section*{WORKED EXAMPLES}

\section*{PART - A}
1. If $\mathrm{A}=\left(\begin{array}{lll}0 & 2 & 3 \\ 2 & 1 & 4\end{array}\right)$; $\mathrm{B}=\left(\begin{array}{lll}7 & 6 & 3 \\ 1 & 4 & 5\end{array}\right)$ find $\mathrm{A}+\mathrm{B}$.

\section*{Solution:}
$$\begin{aligned}
A+B & =\left(\begin{array}{lll}
0 & 2 & 3 \\
2 & 1 & 4
\end{array}\right)+\left(\begin{array}{lll}
7 & 6 & 3 \\
1 & 4 & 5
\end{array}\right) \\
& =\left(\begin{array}{lll}
0+7 & 2+6 & 3+3 \\
2+1 & 1+4 & 4+5
\end{array}\right) \\
A+B & =\left(\begin{array}{lll}
7 & 8 & 6 \\
3 & 5 & 9
\end{array}\right)
\end{aligned}$$
2. If $\mathrm{A}=\left(\begin{array}{ll}1 & 3 \\ 3 & 4\end{array}\right)$; $\mathrm{B}=\left(\begin{array}{ll}1 & 2 \\ 4 & 3\end{array}\right)$ find $\mathrm{A}-\mathrm{B}$.

\section*{Solution:}
$$\begin{aligned}
A-B & =\left(\begin{array}{ll}
1 & 3 \\
3 & 4
\end{array}\right)-\left(\begin{array}{ll}
1 & 2 \\
4 & 3
\end{array}\right) \\
& =\left(\begin{array}{ll}
1-1 & 3-2 \\
3-4 & 4-3
\end{array}\right) \\
A-B & =\left(\begin{array}{cc}
0 & 1 \\
-1 & 1
\end{array}\right)
\end{aligned}$$
3. Prove that the matrix $\left(\begin{array}{ll}2 & -1 \\ 4 & -2\end{array}\right)$ is singular.

\section*{Solution:}
$$\begin{aligned}
& \text { Let } \mathrm{A}=\left(\begin{array}{ll}
2 & -1 \\
4 & -2
\end{array}\right) \\
& \begin{aligned}
|\mathrm{A}| & =\left|\begin{array}{ll}
2 & -1 \\
4 & -2
\end{array}\right| \\
& =-4+4 \\
|\mathrm{~A}| & =0 \quad \therefore \text { A is singular. }
\end{aligned}
\end{aligned}$$
4. Find the rank of $\left(\begin{array}{cc}3 & -4 \\ -6 & 8\end{array}\right)$.

\section*{Solution:}

Let $A=\left(\begin{array}{cc}3 & -4 \\ -6 & 8\end{array}\right)$
Now, $|\mathrm{A}|=\left|\begin{array}{cc}3 & -4 \\ -6 & 8\end{array}\right|=24-24=0 . \quad \therefore \rho(\mathrm{A}) \neq 2$
Since each element of A is non zero,
$\rho(A)=1$.
5. Find the rank of $\left(\begin{array}{lll}1 & 2 & 3 \\ 4 & 5 & 6\end{array}\right)$.

\section*{Solution:}

Let $\mathrm{A}=\left(\begin{array}{lll}1 & 2 & 3 \\ 4 & 5 & 6\end{array}\right)$ and let the sub matrix of $\mathrm{A}=\mathrm{B}=\left|\begin{array}{ll}1 & 2 \\ 4 & 5\end{array}\right|$
$$\begin{aligned}
& |\mathrm{B}|=\left|\begin{array}{ll}
1 & 2 \\
4 & 5
\end{array}\right|=5-8=-3 \neq 0 \\
& \therefore \rho(\mathrm{~A})=2
\end{aligned}$$

\section*{PART - B}
1. If $\mathrm{A}=\left(\begin{array}{ccc}2 & 3 & 0 \\ 5 & 2 & -1\end{array}\right) ; \mathrm{B}=\left(\begin{array}{ccc}3 & -1 & 4 \\ 2 & 6 & 7\end{array}\right)$ find $3 \mathrm{~A}-2 \mathrm{~B}$.

\section*{Solution:}
$$\begin{aligned}
3 A-2 B & =3\left(\begin{array}{ccc}
2 & 3 & 0 \\
5 & 2 & -1
\end{array}\right)-2\left(\begin{array}{ccc}
3 & -1 & 4 \\
2 & 6 & 7
\end{array}\right) \\
& =\left(\begin{array}{ccc}
6 & 9 & 0 \\
15 & 6 & -3
\end{array}\right)-\left(\begin{array}{ccc}
6 & -2 & 8 \\
4 & 12 & 14
\end{array}\right) \\
& =\left(\begin{array}{ccc}
6-6 & 9+2 & 0-8 \\
15-4 & 6-12 & -3-14
\end{array}\right) \\
3 A-2 B & =\left(\begin{array}{ccc}
0 & 11 & -8 \\
11 & -6 & -17
\end{array}\right)
\end{aligned}$$
2. If $\mathrm{f}(x)=4 \mathrm{x}+2$ and $\mathrm{A}=\left(\begin{array}{cc}2 & -1 \\ 0 & 3\end{array}\right)$ find $\mathrm{f}(\mathrm{A})$.

\section*{Solution:}
$$\begin{aligned}
\mathrm{f}(\mathrm{~A}) & =4 \mathrm{~A}+2 \\
& =4 \mathrm{~A}+2 \mathrm{I} \\
& =4\left(\begin{array}{cc}
2 & -1 \\
0 & 3
\end{array}\right)+2\left(\begin{array}{ll}
1 & 0 \\
0 & 1
\end{array}\right) \\
& =\left(\begin{array}{cc}
8 & -4 \\
0 & 12
\end{array}\right)+\left(\begin{array}{ll}
2 & 0 \\
0 & 2
\end{array}\right) \\
\mathrm{f}(\mathrm{~A}) & =\left(\begin{array}{cc}
10 & -4 \\
0 & 14
\end{array}\right)
\end{aligned}$$
3. Find the value of 'a' so that the matrix $\left(\begin{array}{ccc}1 & -2 & 0 \\ 2 & \mathrm{a} & 4 \\ 2 & 1 & 1\end{array}\right)$ is singular.
Solution:

\section*{Solution:}

Let $\mathrm{A}=\left(\begin{array}{ccc}1 & -2 & 0 \\ 2 & \mathrm{a} & 4 \\ 2 & 1 & 1\end{array}\right)$. The matrix is singular, then $|\mathrm{A}|=0$.
i.e $\left|\begin{array}{ccc}1 & -2 & 0 \\ 2 & \mathrm{a} & 4 \\ 2 & 1 & 1\end{array}\right|=0$
expanding through first row
$$\begin{aligned}
& 1(a-4)+2(2-8)+0=0 \\
& a-4+2(-6)=0 \\
& a-4-12=0 \\
& a-16=0 \\
& a=16
\end{aligned}$$
4. If $\mathrm{A}=\left(\begin{array}{ll}1 & 0 \\ 2 & 1\end{array}\right)$ and $\mathrm{B}=\left(\begin{array}{ll}0 & 3 \\ 2 & 4\end{array}\right)$ find AB .

\section*{Solution:}
$$\begin{aligned}
& \mathrm{AB}=\left(\begin{array}{ll}
1 & 0 \\
2 & 1
\end{array}\right)\left(\begin{array}{ll}
0 & 3 \\
2 & 4
\end{array}\right) \\
& \mathrm{AB}=\left(\begin{array}{ll}
0+0 & 3+0 \\
0+2 & 6+4
\end{array}\right)=\left(\begin{array}{cc}
0 & 3 \\
2 & 10
\end{array}\right)
\end{aligned}$$
5. Find the inverse of $\left(\begin{array}{ll}2 & 3 \\ 4 & 5\end{array}\right)$.

\section*{Solution:}
$$\begin{aligned}
& \text { Let } \mathrm{A}=\left(\begin{array}{ll}
2 & 3 \\
4 & 5
\end{array}\right) \therefore|\mathrm{A}|=\left|\begin{array}{ll}
2 & 3 \\
4 & 5
\end{array}\right|=10-12=-2 \neq 0 \\
& \text { Adj } \mathrm{A}=\left(\begin{array}{cc}
5 & -3 \\
-4 & 2
\end{array}\right) \\
& \mathrm{A}^{-1}=\frac{1}{|\mathrm{~A}|} \text { Adj } \mathrm{A}=\frac{1}{-2}\left(\begin{array}{cc}
5 & -3 \\
-4 & 2
\end{array}\right)
\end{aligned}$$
6. Find the rank of $\left(\begin{array}{cc}5 & -15 \\ -6 & 18 \\ 7 & -21\end{array}\right)$.
Solution:
$$\begin{aligned}
& \text { Let } \mathrm{A}=\left(\begin{array}{cc}
5 & -15 \\
-6 & 18 \\
7 & -21
\end{array}\right) \\
& \left|\begin{array}{cc}
5 & -15 \\
-6 & 18
\end{array}\right|=90-90=0 \\
& \left|\begin{array}{cc}
-6 & -15 \\
7 & -21
\end{array}\right|
\end{aligned}=-105+105=0 \quad \begin{aligned}
&\left|\begin{array}{cc}
6 & 18 \\
7 & 21
\end{array}\right|=126-126=0 \quad \therefore \rho(\mathrm{~A}) \neq 2 \\
& \therefore \rho(\mathrm{~A})=1
\end{aligned}$$

\section*{PART - C}
1. If $\mathrm{A}=\left[\begin{array}{ccc}2 & -1 & 0 \\ 0 & -2 & 1 \\ 1 & 0 & 1\end{array}\right]$ and $\mathrm{B}=\left[\begin{array}{ccc}-2 & 1 & -1 \\ 1 & 2 & -2 \\ 2 & -1 & -4\end{array}\right]$ show that $\mathrm{AB}=\mathrm{BA}$.

\section*{Solution:}
$$\mathrm{AB}=\left[\begin{array}{ccc}
2 & -1 & 0 \\
0 & -2 & 1 \\
1 & 0 & 1
\end{array}\right]\left[\begin{array}{ccc}
-2 & 1 & -1 \\
1 & 2 & -2 \\
2 & -1 & -4
\end{array}\right]$$
$$\begin{aligned}
&=\left[\begin{array}{ccc}
-4-1+0 & 2-2+0 & -2+2+0 \\
0-2+2 & 0-4-1 & 0+4-4 \\
-2+0+2 & 1+0-1 & -1+0-4
\end{array}\right] \\
&=\left[\begin{array}{ccc}
-5 & 0 & 0 \\
0 & -5 & 0 \\
0 & 0 & -5
\end{array}\right] \\
& \text { BA }=\left[\begin{array}{ccc}
-2 & 1 & -1 \\
1 & 2 & -2 \\
2 & -1 & -4
\end{array}\right]\left[\begin{array}{ccc}
2 & -1 & 0 \\
0 & -2 & 1 \\
1 & 0 & 1
\end{array}\right] \\
&=\left[\begin{array}{ccc}
-4+0-1 & 2-2+0 & 0+1-1 \\
2+0-2 & -1-4+0 & 0+2-2 \\
4+0-4 & -2+2+0 & 0-1-4
\end{array}\right] \\
& \mathrm{BA}=\left[\begin{array}{ccc}
-5 & 0 & 0 \\
0 & -5 & 0 \\
0 & 0 & -5
\end{array}\right] \\
& \therefore \mathrm{AB}=\mathrm{BA}
\end{aligned}$$
2. Show that $\mathrm{AB} \neq \mathrm{BA}$ if $\mathrm{A}=\left[\begin{array}{cc}1 & 2 \\ -1 & 4\end{array}\right]$ and $\mathrm{B}=\left[\begin{array}{cc}3 & 2 \\ 1 & -1\end{array}\right]$.

Solution:
$$\begin{aligned}
& \mathrm{AB}=\left[\begin{array}{cc}
1 & 2 \\
-1 & 4
\end{array}\right]\left[\begin{array}{cc}
3 & 2 \\
1 & -1
\end{array}\right] \\
& \mathrm{AB}=\left[\begin{array}{cc}
3+2 & 2-2 \\
-3+4 & -2-4
\end{array}\right]=\left[\begin{array}{cc}
5 & 0 \\
1 & -6
\end{array}\right] \\
& \mathrm{BA}=\left[\begin{array}{cc}
3 & 2 \\
1 & -1
\end{array}\right]\left[\begin{array}{cc}
1 & 2 \\
-1 & 4
\end{array}\right]=\left[\begin{array}{cc}
3-2 & 6+8 \\
1+1 & 2-4
\end{array}\right] \\
& \mathrm{BA}=\left[\begin{array}{cc}
1 & 14 \\
2 & -2
\end{array}\right]=\left[\begin{array}{cc}
1 & 14 \\
2 & -2
\end{array}\right] \\
& \therefore \mathrm{AB} \neq \mathrm{BA}
\end{aligned}$$
3. If $\mathrm{A}=\left[\begin{array}{ll}1 & 2 \\ 3 & 1\end{array}\right]$ and if $\mathrm{f}(x)=3 x^{2}-2 x+4$ find $\mathrm{f}(\mathrm{A})$.

\section*{Solution:}
$$\text { If } \begin{aligned}
& \mathrm{f}(x)=3 x^{2}-2 x+4 \\
& \mathrm{f}(\mathrm{~A})=3 \mathrm{~A}^{2}-2 \mathrm{~A}+4 \mathrm{I} \\
\mathrm{~A}^{2} & =\mathrm{A} \times \mathrm{A} \\
& =\left[\begin{array}{ll}
1 & 2 \\
3 & 1
\end{array}\right]\left[\begin{array}{ll}
1 & 2 \\
3 & 1
\end{array}\right] \\
& =\left[\begin{array}{ll}
1+6 & 2+2 \\
3+3 & 6+1
\end{array}\right] \\
\mathrm{A}^{2} & =\left[\begin{array}{ll}
7 & 4 \\
6 & 7
\end{array}\right]
\end{aligned}$$
$$\begin{aligned}
& \therefore \mathrm{f}(\mathrm{~A})=3 \mathrm{~A}^{2}-2 \mathrm{~A}+4 \mathrm{I} \\
& \\
& =3\left[\begin{array}{ll}
7 & 4 \\
6 & 7
\end{array}\right]-2\left[\begin{array}{ll}
1 & 2 \\
3 & 1
\end{array}\right]+4\left[\begin{array}{ll}
1 & 0 \\
0 & 1
\end{array}\right] \\
& \\
& =\left[\begin{array}{ll}
21 & 12 \\
18 & 21
\end{array}\right]+\left[\begin{array}{cc}
-2 & -4 \\
-6 & -2
\end{array}\right]+\left[\begin{array}{ll}
4 & 0 \\
0 & 4
\end{array}\right] \\
& \mathrm{f}(\mathrm{~A})
\end{aligned}=\left[\begin{array}{cc}
21-2+4 & 12-4+0 \\
18-6+0 & 21-2+4
\end{array}\right]=\left[\begin{array}{cc}
23 & 8 \\
12 & 23
\end{array}\right] .8 . ~ \$$$
4. Find the inverse of the matrix $\left[\begin{array}{ccc}1 & 1 & -1 \\ 2 & 1 & 0 \\ -1 & 2 & 3\end{array}\right]$.
Solution:
$$\begin{aligned}
& \text { Let } A=\left[\begin{array}{ccc}
1 & 1 & -1 \\
2 & 1 & 0 \\
-1 & 2 & 3
\end{array}\right] \\
& |\mathrm{A}|=\left|\begin{array}{ccc}
1 & 1 & -1 \\
2 & 1 & 0 \\
-1 & 2 & 3
\end{array}\right| \\
& =1(3-0)-1(6-0)-1(4+1)=1(3)-1(6)-1(5) \\
& =3-6-5=-8 \neq 0 \mathrm{~A}^{-1} \text { exists }
\end{aligned}$$

Co-factors
$$\begin{aligned}
& \mathrm{A}_{11}=+\left|\begin{array}{ll}
1 & 0 \\
2 & 3
\end{array}\right|=3-0=3 \\
& \mathrm{~A}_{12}=-\left|\begin{array}{cc}
2 & 0 \\
-1 & 3
\end{array}\right|=-(6-0)=-6 \\
& \mathrm{~A}_{13}=+\left|\begin{array}{cc}
2 & 1 \\
-1 & 2
\end{array}\right|=(4+1)=5 \\
& \mathrm{~A}_{21}=-\left|\begin{array}{cc}
1 & -1 \\
2 & 3
\end{array}\right|=-(3+2)=-5 \\
& \mathrm{~A}_{22}=+\left|\begin{array}{cc}
1 & -1 \\
-1 & 3
\end{array}\right|=(3-1)=2 \\
& \mathrm{~A}_{23}=-\left|\begin{array}{cc}
1 & 1 \\
-1 & 2
\end{array}\right|=-(2+1)=-3 \\
& \mathrm{~A}_{31}=+\left|\begin{array}{cc}
1 & -1 \\
1 & 0
\end{array}\right|=(0+1)=1 \\
& \mathrm{~A}_{32}=-\left|\begin{array}{cc}
1 & -1 \\
2 & 0
\end{array}\right|=-(0+2)=-2 \\
& \mathrm{~A}_{32}=+\left|\begin{array}{cc}
1 & 1 \\
2 & 1
\end{array}\right|=(1-2)=-1
\end{aligned}$$

Co-factor matrix $A=\left[\begin{array}{ccc}3 & -6 & 5 \\ -5 & 2 & -3 \\ 1 & -2 & -1\end{array}\right]$

Adj. $A=\left[\begin{array}{ccc}3 & -5 & 1 \\ -6 & 2 & -2 \\ 5 & -3 & -1\end{array}\right]$
$$\therefore \mathrm{A}^{-1}=\frac{\operatorname{Adj} \mathrm{A}}{|\mathrm{~A}|}=\frac{-1}{8}\left[\begin{array}{ccc}
3 & -5 & 1 \\
-6 & 2 & -2 \\
5 & -3 & -1
\end{array}\right]$$
5. If $\mathrm{A}=\left[\begin{array}{cc}2 & 3 \\ -1 & 0\end{array}\right] ; \mathrm{B}=\left[\begin{array}{cc}-1 & 2 \\ 0 & 1\end{array}\right]$ show that $(\mathrm{AB})^{-1}=\mathrm{B}^{-1} \mathrm{~A}^{-1}$.

\section*{Solution:}
$$\begin{aligned}
\mathrm{AB} & =\left[\begin{array}{cc}
2 & 3 \\
-1 & 0
\end{array}\right]\left[\begin{array}{cc}
-1 & 2 \\
0 & 1
\end{array}\right] \\
\mathrm{AB} & =\left[\begin{array}{cc}
-2 & 7 \\
1 & -2
\end{array}\right] \\
|\mathrm{AB}| & =\left|\begin{array}{cc}
-2 & 7 \\
1 & -2
\end{array}\right|=-3 \neq 0
\end{aligned}$$
$(\mathrm{AB})^{-1}$ exists
$$\begin{aligned}
\operatorname{Adj}(\mathrm{AB}) & =\left(\begin{array}{ll}
-2 & -7 \\
-1 & -2
\end{array}\right) \\
(\mathrm{AB})^{-1} & =\frac{1}{-3}\left(\begin{array}{ll}
-2 & -7 \\
-1 & -2
\end{array}\right) \\
(\mathrm{AB})^{-1} & =\frac{-1}{3}\left(\begin{array}{ll}
-2 & -7 \\
-1 & -2
\end{array}\right)
\end{aligned}$$
$\mathrm{B}=\left(\begin{array}{cc}-1 & 2 \\ 0 & 1\end{array}\right) \quad|\mathrm{B}|=\left|\begin{array}{cc}-1 & 2 \\ 0 & 1\end{array}\right|=-1 \neq 0$
$\operatorname{Adj} B=\left(\begin{array}{cc}-1 & 2 \\ 0 & 1\end{array}\right)$
$$\begin{aligned}
\mathrm{B}^{-1} & =\frac{1}{-1}\left(\begin{array}{cc}
-1 & 2 \\
0 & 1
\end{array}\right) \\
\mathrm{A} & =\left[\begin{array}{cc}
2 & 3 \\
-1 & 0
\end{array}\right] \quad|\mathrm{A}|=\left|\begin{array}{cc}
2 & 3 \\
-1 & 0
\end{array}\right|=3 \neq 0 \\
\text { Adj } \mathrm{A} & =\left[\begin{array}{cc}
0 & -3 \\
1 & 2
\end{array}\right] \\
\mathrm{A}^{-1} & =\frac{1}{3}\left[\begin{array}{ll}
0 & -3 \\
1 & 2
\end{array}\right] \\
\mathrm{B}^{-1} \mathrm{~A}^{-1} & =-\frac{1}{1}\left[\begin{array}{ll}
1 & -2 \\
0 & -1
\end{array}\right] \times \frac{1}{3}\left[\begin{array}{cc}
0 & -3 \\
1 & 2
\end{array}\right] \\
\mathrm{B}^{-1} \mathrm{~A}^{-1} & =-\frac{1}{3}\left[\begin{array}{cc}
1 & -2 \\
0 & -1
\end{array}\right]\left[\begin{array}{cc}
0 & -3 \\
1 & 2
\end{array}\right] \\
\mathrm{B}^{-1} \mathrm{~A}^{-1} & =-\frac{1}{3}\left[\begin{array}{cc}
-2 & -7 \\
-1 & -2
\end{array}\right]
\end{aligned}$$

From (i) and (ii) (AB) $)^{-1}=\mathrm{B}^{-1} \mathrm{~A}^{-1}$
6. Find the rank of the matrix $\left[\begin{array}{cccc}2 & 3 & 4 & -1 \\ 5 & 2 & 0 & -1 \\ -4 & 5 & 12 & -1\end{array}\right]$.

\section*{Solution:}

Here the rank of the matrix can not exceed 3, the minimum of 3 and 4 .
The value of the each of the determinants.
$$\begin{aligned}
& \left|\begin{array}{ccc}
2 & 3 & 4 \\
5 & 2 & 0 \\
-4 & 5 & 12
\end{array}\right|=2(24-0)-3(60-0)+4(25+8)=48-180+132=0 \\
& \left|\begin{array}{ccc}
3 & 4 & -1 \\
2 & 0 & -1 \\
5 & 12 & -1
\end{array}\right|=0 \\
& \text { Smilarly we can prove }\left|\begin{array}{ccc}
2 & 4 & -1 \\
5 & 0 & -1 \\
-4 & 12 & -1
\end{array}\right|=0 \text { and }\left|\begin{array}{ccc}
2 & 3 & -1 \\
5 & 2 & -1 \\
-4 & 5 & -1
\end{array}\right|=0
\end{aligned}$$

While the second order determinant
$$\left|\begin{array}{ll}
2 & 3 \\
5 & 2
\end{array}\right|=4-15=-11 \neq 0$$

Hence the rank of the matrix $=2$

\subsection*{1.3 BINOMIAL THEOREM}

\section*{Definition of Factorial Notation:}

The Continued Product of first ' $n$ ' natural numbers is called $n$ factorial and is denoted by $n$ ! or $\lfloor\mathrm{n}$.
$$\text { i.e } \begin{aligned}
\underline{\mathrm{n}} & =1 \times 2 \times 3 \times \ldots \ldots . .(\mathrm{n}-1) \times \mathrm{n} \\
\underline{5} & =1 \times 2 \times 3 \times 4 \times 5 \\
\underline{5} & =120
\end{aligned}$$

\section*{Zero factorial:}

We will require zero factorial for calculating any value which contains zero factorial. It does not make any sense to define it as the product of the integers from 1 to zero. So we define $\lfloor=1$.

\section*{Definition of Permutation and Combination:}

First we begin with the following problem. We want to select 2 Carrom players from among 5 players. Let us denote them by the letters A, B, C, D and E.

To select 2 players, we shall first take $A$ and then with him, associate $B, C, D$ and $E$. That is $A B, A C$, AD and AE are four types of selection of 2 players. Also starting with B , we have $\mathrm{BC}, \mathrm{BD}$ and BE , with $\mathrm{C}, \mathrm{CD}$ and CE , and finally starting with D , we have DE only.

So, totally there are 10 ways. If we denote the number of ways of selection of 2 players out of 5 players, symbolically denoted by $5 \mathrm{C}_{2}=10$.

Also, let us assume that the selected 2 players want to give pose for a photo. Two chairs are brought as shown below, there are two types of arrangements.
\begin{tabular}{cccc}
\multicolumn{2}{c}{ Arrangement -1} & \multicolumn{2}{c}{ Arrangement -2} \\
A & B & B & A \\
Chair -1 & Chair -2 & Chair -1 & Chair -2
\end{tabular}

Thus we see that for one selection, there are two different arrangements, and so for the total of 10 selection, the total number of arrangements is 20 .

That is if the number of ways of arrangement of 2 players out of 5 players is denoted by $5 \mathrm{P}_{2}$. We have $5 \mathrm{P}_{2}=20$.

The above "arrangement" and "selection" are usually called "permutation" and "combination".
Where $\mathrm{nP}_{\mathrm{r}}$ is the number of ways of arrangement (or permutation) of " r " things out of ' n ' things. Also $\mathrm{nC}_{\mathrm{r}}$ is the number of ways of selection (or combination) of "r" things out of " $n$ " things.

Note: The value of $\mathrm{nP}_{\mathrm{r}}$ and $\mathrm{nC}_{\mathrm{r}}$ are given below.
$$\begin{aligned}
& \mathrm{nP}_{\mathrm{r}}=\frac{\underline{\mathrm{n}}}{\underline{\mathrm{n}-\mathrm{r}}} \text { (or) } \frac{\mathrm{n}!}{(\mathrm{n}-\mathrm{r})!} \\
& \mathrm{nC}_{\mathrm{r}}=\frac{\mathrm{n}!}{(\mathrm{n}-\mathrm{r})!\mathrm{r}!}
\end{aligned}$$

\section*{BINOMIAL THEOREM}

Binomial means an expression, consist of two numbers (or) terms connected by plus sign or minus sign.

\section*{Example:}
$$x+\mathrm{y} ; 2 x-\mathrm{y} ; x^{3}-\frac{1}{x} \text { etc. }$$

In binomial theorem, we deal with the powers of binomial expressions. From School Studies, we know that
$$\begin{aligned}
& (x+\mathrm{a})^{2}=x^{2}+2 x \mathrm{a}+\mathrm{a}^{2} \text { and } \\
& (x+\mathrm{a})^{3}=x^{3}+3 x^{2} \mathrm{a}+3 x \mathrm{a}^{2}+\mathrm{a}^{3}
\end{aligned}$$
we can write the expansion of $(x+\mathrm{a})^{3}$ as below
$(x+\mathrm{a})^{3}=3 \mathrm{C}_{0} x^{3} \mathrm{a}^{0}+3 \mathrm{C}_{1} x^{2} \mathrm{a}^{1}+3 \mathrm{C}_{2} x^{1} \mathrm{a}^{2}+3 \mathrm{C}_{3} x^{0} \mathrm{a}^{3}$.
Where $3 \mathrm{C}_{0}=3 \mathrm{C}_{3}=1 \quad \mathrm{nC}_{\mathrm{r}}=\mathrm{nC}_{\mathrm{n}-\mathrm{r}}$
Similarly for $(x+\mathrm{a})^{4},(x+\mathrm{a})^{5}$ can be expanded as mentioned above.

\section*{Binomial theorem for a positive integral index:}

If " n " is any positive integer, then $(x+\mathrm{a})^{\mathrm{n}}=x^{\mathrm{n}}+\mathrm{nC}_{1} x^{\mathrm{n}-1} \mathrm{a}+\mathrm{nC}_{2} x^{\mathrm{n}-2} \mathrm{a}^{2}+\ldots .+\mathrm{nC}_{\mathrm{r}} x^{\mathrm{nr}} \mathrm{a}^{\mathrm{r}}+\ldots \ldots . .+\mathrm{a}^{\mathrm{n}}$.

\section*{Note:}
1. The total number of terms in the expansion is $(\mathrm{n}+1)$.
2. In each term, sum of the powers of $x$ and $a$ is equal to $n$.
3. The general term of $(x+a)^{\mathrm{n}}$ is $\mathrm{T}_{\mathrm{r}+1}=\mathrm{nC}_{\mathrm{r}} x^{\mathrm{nr} \mathrm{r}} \mathrm{a}^{\mathrm{r}} \quad$ i.e $(\mathrm{r}+1)^{\text {th }}$ term
4. If the power ' $n$ ' is an even integer, there is only one middle term which is at $\left(\frac{n}{2}+1\right)^{\text {th }}$ place and if ' $n$ ' is odd integer there are two middle terms, which are at $\left(\frac{\mathrm{n}+1}{2}\right)^{\text {th }}$ and $\left(\frac{\mathrm{n}+3}{2}\right)^{\text {th }}$ places.

\section*{Binomial Theorem for rational index:}

If $x$ is numerically less than one and $n$ any rational number, then
$$(1+x)^{\mathrm{n}}=1+\mathrm{n} x+\frac{\mathrm{n}(\mathrm{n}-1)}{1 \times 2} x^{2}+\frac{\mathrm{n}(\mathrm{n}-1)(\mathrm{n}-2)}{1 \times 2 \times 3} x^{3}+.$$
$\qquad$
Note:
1. The number of terms in the expansion is infinite.
2. Here the notations $\mathrm{nC}_{0}, \mathrm{nC}_{1}, \mathrm{nC}_{2}$ $\qquad$ etc are meaningless. Since $n$ is a rational number.
3. Also here we consider, is expansions for negative integers upto -3 . When the values of ' $n$ ' are $-1,-2,-3$ the expansions are
(i) $(1-x)^{-1}=1+x+x^{2}+x^{3}+$ $\qquad$
(ii) $(1+x)^{-1}=1-x+x^{2}-x^{3}+$ $\qquad$
(iii) $(1-x)^{-2}=1+2 x+3 x^{2}+4 x^{3}+$ $\qquad$
(iv) $(1+x)^{-2}=1-2 x+3 x^{2}-4 x^{3}+$ $\qquad$
(v) $(1-x)^{-3}=1+3 x+6 x^{2}+10 x^{3}+$ $\qquad$
(vi) $(1+x)^{-3}=1-3 x+6 x^{2}-10 x^{3}+$ $\qquad$

\section*{WORKED EXAMPLES}

\section*{PART -A}
1. Write down the number of terms in $\left(x^{2}+\frac{2}{x}\right)^{11}$.

\section*{Solution:}

The number of terms is $\mathrm{n}+1=11+1=12$
2. Which term in $(3 x-y)^{6}$ is the middle term.

\section*{Solution:}

Here $\mathrm{n}=6$ (even)
$$\begin{aligned}
\text { Middle term } & =\left(\frac{\mathrm{n}}{2}+1\right) \\
& =\left(\frac{6}{2}+1\right)=3+1 \\
& =4^{\text {th }} \text { term }
\end{aligned}$$
$\therefore$ 4th term is the middle term.
3. Write down the expansion for $(1-x)^{-1}$.

\section*{Solution:}
$$(1-x)^{-1}=1+x+x^{2}+x^{3}+$$
$\qquad$
4. Expand by using Binomial Theorem $(3 x-2 y)^{3}$.

\section*{Solution:}
$$(3 x-2 \mathrm{y})^{3}=(3 x)^{3}+3 \mathrm{C}_{1}(3 x)^{2}(-2 \mathrm{y})^{1}+3 \mathrm{C}_{2}(3 x)^{1}(-2 \mathrm{y})^{2}+(-2 \mathrm{y})^{3}$$
5. Which are the two middle terms in $\left(2 x^{2}+\frac{3}{x}\right)^{13}$.

\section*{Solution:}

Here $\mathrm{n}=13$ (odd)
Middle terms are $\left(\frac{\mathrm{n}+1}{2}\right)^{\text {th }}$ and $\left(\frac{\mathrm{n}+3}{2}\right)^{\text {th }}$. i.e $\frac{13+1}{2}=\frac{14}{2}=7$ and
$$\frac{13+3}{2}=\frac{16}{2}=8$$
$\therefore 7^{\text {th }}$ and $8^{\text {th }}$ terms are two middle terms.

\section*{PART - B}
1. Find the general term in the expansion of $\left(x+\frac{1}{2 x}\right)^{7}$.

\section*{Solution:}

Let $\left(x+\frac{1}{2 x}\right)^{7}=(\mathrm{X}+\mathrm{A})^{\mathrm{n}}$
Where $\mathrm{X}=\mathrm{x} ; \mathrm{n}=7, \quad \mathrm{~A}=\frac{1}{2 x}$
general term $\mathrm{T}_{\mathrm{r}+1}=\mathrm{nC}_{\mathrm{r}} \mathrm{X}^{\mathrm{n}-\mathrm{r}} \mathrm{A}^{\mathrm{r}}$
i.e $\mathrm{T}_{\mathrm{r}+1}=7 \mathrm{C}_{\mathrm{r}} \mathrm{x}^{7-\mathrm{r}}\left(\frac{1}{2 x}\right)^{\mathrm{r}}=7 \mathrm{C}_{\mathrm{r}} \mathrm{x}^{7-\mathrm{r}} \frac{1}{2^{\mathrm{r}} \mathrm{x}^{\mathrm{r}}}=7 \mathrm{C}_{\mathrm{r}} \frac{\mathrm{x}^{7-2 \mathrm{r}}}{2^{\mathrm{r}}}$
2. Find the 5 th term in the expansion of $\left(x+\frac{1}{x}\right)^{8}$.

\section*{Solution:}

The general term $T_{r+1}=n C_{r} x^{n-r} a^{r}$
Put $\mathrm{r}=4$, we get 5 th term $\mathrm{T}_{5}$. Also $x=x, \mathrm{a}=\frac{1}{x}, \mathrm{n}=8$.
$$\begin{aligned}
\mathrm{T}_{5} & =8 \mathrm{C}_{4} x^{8-4}\left(\frac{1}{x}\right)^{4} \\
& =8 \mathrm{C}_{4} x^{4}\left(\frac{1}{x}\right)^{4} \\
& =8 \mathrm{C}_{4} x^{4} \frac{1}{x^{4}} \\
\mathrm{~T}_{5} & =8 \mathrm{C}_{4}
\end{aligned}$$
3. Expand in higher powers of $x$ upto three terms $(1+3 x)^{-2}$.

\section*{Solution:}
$$(1+x)^{-2}=1-2 x+3 x^{2}-4 x^{3}+\ldots \ldots \ldots . .$$
where $x=3 x$ only
$$\begin{aligned}
\therefore(1+3 x)^{-2} & =1-2(3 x)+3(3 x)^{2}-4(3 x)^{3} \\
& =1-6 x+27 x^{2}-108 x^{3}+\ldots \ldots . . .
\end{aligned}$$

\section*{PART - C}
1. Find the middle terms in the expansion of $\left(2 x^{2}+\frac{1}{x}\right)^{11}$.

\section*{Solution:}

Since here ' $n$ ' is odd number, the total number of terms is even and so there are two middle terms.
$\left(\frac{\mathrm{n}+1}{2}\right)^{\text {th }}$ and $\left(\frac{\mathrm{n}+3}{2}\right)^{\text {th }}$ terms are middle terms.
$\left(\frac{11+1}{2}\right)^{\text {th }}$ and $\left(\frac{11+3}{2}\right)^{\text {th }}$ i.e $6^{\text {th }}$ and $7^{\text {th }}$ terms.
Put $\mathrm{r}=5$ and 6 in the general term.
Now, general term is $\mathrm{T}_{\mathrm{r}+1}=\mathrm{nC}_{\mathrm{r}} x^{\mathrm{n}-\mathrm{r}} \mathrm{a}^{\mathrm{r}}$
$$\begin{aligned}
\mathrm{T}_{5+1} & =11 \mathrm{C}_{5}\left(2 x^{2}\right)^{11-5}\left(\frac{1}{x}\right)^{5} \\
\mathrm{~T}_{6} & =11 \mathrm{C}_{5}\left(2 \mathrm{x}^{2}\right)^{6}\left(\frac{1}{x}\right)^{5}=11 \mathrm{C}_{5} 2^{6} x^{12} \frac{1}{x^{5}}=11 \mathrm{C}_{5} 2^{6} x^{7}
\end{aligned}$$

Put $\mathrm{r}=6$
$$\begin{aligned}
\mathrm{T}_{6+1} & =11 \mathrm{C}_{6}\left(2 x^{2}\right)^{11-6}\left(\frac{1}{x}\right)^{6} \\
\mathrm{~T}_{7} & =11 \mathrm{C}_{6}\left(2 \mathrm{x}^{2}\right)^{5}\left(\frac{1}{x}\right)^{6}=11 \mathrm{C}_{6} 2^{5} \mathrm{x}^{10} \frac{1}{\mathrm{x}^{6}}=11 \mathrm{C}_{6} 2^{5} \mathrm{x}^{4}
\end{aligned}$$
2. Find the co-efficient of $x^{32}$ in the expansion of $\left(x^{4}-\frac{1}{x^{3}}\right)^{15}$.

\section*{Solution:}

Now, general terms is $\mathrm{T}_{\mathrm{r}+1}=\mathrm{nC}_{\mathrm{r}} x^{\mathrm{nr}} \mathrm{a}^{\mathrm{r}}$
$$\begin{aligned}
\mathrm{T}_{\mathrm{r}+1} & =15 \mathrm{C}_{\mathrm{r}}\left(x^{4}\right)^{15-\mathrm{r}}\left(\frac{-1}{x^{3}}\right)^{\mathrm{r}} \\
& =15 \mathrm{C}_{\mathrm{r}} x^{60-4 \mathrm{r}}(-1)^{\mathrm{r}} x^{-3 \mathrm{r}} \\
\mathrm{~T}_{\mathrm{r}+1} & =15 \mathrm{C}_{\mathrm{r}}(-1)^{\mathrm{r}} x^{60-7 \mathrm{r}} \quad .
\end{aligned}$$

To find the co-efficient of $x^{32}$, put
$$\begin{aligned}
32 & =60-7 \mathrm{r} \\
7 \mathrm{r} & =60-32 \\
7 \mathrm{r} & =28 \\
\mathrm{r} & =\frac{28}{7} \\
\mathrm{r} & =4
\end{aligned}$$

Applying $\mathrm{r}=4$ in the equation (i), we get
$$\begin{aligned}
& \mathrm{T}_{4+1}=15 \mathrm{C}_{4}(-1)^{4} x^{60-7(4)} \\
& \mathrm{T}_{5}=15 \mathrm{C}_{4} x^{32}
\end{aligned}$$
$\therefore$ co-efficient of $x^{32}$ is $15 \mathrm{C}_{4}$.
3. Find the term independent of $x$ in the expansion of $\left(x-\frac{1}{x}\right)^{10}$.

\section*{Solution:}

Now, general term $\mathrm{T}_{\mathrm{r}+1}=\mathrm{nC}_{\mathrm{r}} x^{\mathrm{nr}} \mathrm{a}^{\mathrm{r}}$
$$\begin{aligned}
\mathrm{T}_{\mathrm{r}+1} & =10 \mathrm{C}_{\mathrm{r}} x^{10-\mathrm{r}}\left(\frac{-1}{x}\right)^{\mathrm{r}} \\
& =10 \mathrm{C}_{\mathrm{r}} 1^{10-\mathrm{r}}(-1)^{\mathrm{r}} x^{-\mathrm{r}} \\
\mathrm{~T}_{\mathrm{r}+1} & =10 \mathrm{C}_{\mathrm{r}}(-1)^{\mathrm{r}} x^{10-2 \mathrm{r}} \ldots
\end{aligned}$$

To find independent term, put $10-2 \mathrm{r}=0$
i.e $10-2 \mathrm{r}=0$
$$\begin{aligned}
10 & =2 \mathrm{r} \\
5 & =\mathrm{r}, \quad \mathrm{r}=5
\end{aligned}$$

Put $\mathrm{r}=5$ in equation (i) we get
$$\begin{aligned}
\mathrm{T}_{5+1} & =10 \mathrm{C}_{5}(-1)^{5} x^{10-2(5)} \\
& =10 \mathrm{C}_{5}(-1) x^{0} \\
\mathrm{~T}_{6} & =-10 \mathrm{C}_{5} \text { i.e The term independent of } \mathrm{x} \text { is }-10 \mathrm{C}_{5} .
\end{aligned}$$
4. Write the first three terms in the expansion of $(3-4 x)^{-3}$.

\section*{Solution:}
$$\begin{aligned}
(3-4 x)^{-3} & =3^{-3}\left(1-\frac{4}{3} x\right)^{-3} \\
& =\frac{1}{3^{3}}\left[1+(-3)\left(-\frac{4}{3} x\right)+\frac{(-3)(-3-1)}{1.2}\left(\frac{-4}{3} x\right)^{2}+\ldots \ldots \ldots . .\right] \\
& =\frac{1}{27}\left[1+4 x+\frac{(-3)(-4)}{2}\left(\frac{16 x^{2}}{9}\right)+\ldots \ldots \ldots .\right] \\
& =\frac{1}{27}\left[1+4 x+\frac{32 x^{2}}{3}+\ldots \ldots . .\right]
\end{aligned}$$

\subsection*{2.1 ALGEBRA OF COMPLEX NUMBERS:}

Definition - Real and Imaginary parts, Conjugates, Modulus and amplitude form, Polar form of a complex number, multiplication and division of complex numbers (geometrical proof not needed) - Simple Problems. Argan Diagram - Collinear points, four points forming square, rectangle, rhombus and parallelogram only. Simple problems.
