Demoivre's Theorem (Statement only) - related simple problems.

\subsection*{2.3 ROOTS OF COMPLEX NUMBERS}

Finding the $n$th roots of unity - solving equation of the form $\mathrm{x}^{\mathrm{n}} \pm 1=0$ where $\mathrm{n} \leq 7$. Simple problems.

\subsection*{2.1 ALGEBRA OF COMPLEX NUMBERS}

\section*{Introduction:}

Let us consider the quadratic equation $a x^{2}+b x+c=0$. The solution of this equation is given by the formula $x=\frac{-\mathrm{b} \pm \sqrt{\mathrm{b}^{2}-4 \mathrm{ac}}}{2 \mathrm{a}}$ which is meaningful only when $\mathrm{b}^{2}-4 \mathrm{ac}>0$. Because the square of a real number is always positive and it cannot be negative. If it is negative, then the solution for the equation extends the real number system to a new kind of number system that allows the square root of negative numbers. The square root of -1 is denoted by the symbol $i$, called the imaginary unit, which was first introduced in mathematics by the famous Swiss mathematician, Leonhard Euler in 1748. Thus for any two real numbers $a$ and $b$, we can form a new number $a+i b$ is called a complex number. The set of all complex numbers denoted by C and the nomenclature of a complex number was introduced by a German mathematician C.F. Gauss.

\section*{Definition: Complex Number}

A number which is of the form $\mathrm{a}+\mathrm{ib}$ where $\mathrm{a}, \mathrm{b} \in \mathrm{R}$ and $\mathrm{i}^{2}=-1$ is called a complex number and it is denoted by z . If $\mathrm{z}=\mathrm{a}+\mathrm{ib}$ then $a$ is called the real part of z and $b$ is called the imaginary part of z and are denoted by $\operatorname{Re}(z)$ and $\operatorname{Im}(z)$.

For example, if $\mathrm{z}=3+4 \mathrm{i}$ then $\operatorname{Re}(\mathrm{z})=3$ and $\operatorname{Im}(\mathrm{z})=4$.

\section*{Note:}

In the complex number $\mathrm{z}=\mathrm{a}+\mathrm{ib}$ we have,
(i) If $a=0$ then $z$ is purely imaginary
(ii) If $b=0$ then $z$ is purely real.
(iii) $\mathrm{z}=\mathrm{a}+\mathrm{ib}=(\mathrm{a}, \mathrm{b})$ any complex number can be expressed as an ordered pair.

\section*{Conjugate of a complex number:}

If $z=a+i b$ then the conjugate of $z$ is defined by $a-i b$ and it is denoted by $\bar{z}$. Thus, if $z=a+i b$ then $\bar{z}=a-i b$.

\section*{Results:}
(i) $\overline{\mathrm{Z}}=\mathrm{Z}$
(ii) $\mathrm{a}=\operatorname{Re}(\mathrm{z})=\frac{\mathrm{z}+\overline{\mathrm{z}}}{2} \& \mathrm{~b}=\mathrm{I}_{\mathrm{m}}(\mathrm{z})=\frac{\mathrm{z}-\overline{\mathrm{z}}}{2}$
(iii) $\overline{\mathrm{Z}_{1}+\mathrm{Z}_{2}}=\overline{\mathrm{Z}}_{1}+\overline{\mathrm{Z}}_{2}$
(iv) $\overline{\mathrm{Z}_{1}-\mathrm{Z}_{2}}=\overline{\mathrm{Z}}_{1}-\overline{\mathrm{Z}}_{2}$
(v) $\overline{\mathrm{Z}_{1} \mathrm{Z}_{2}}=\overline{\mathrm{Z}}_{1} \overline{\mathrm{Z}}_{2}$
(vi) $\left(\frac{z_{1}}{z_{2}}\right)=\frac{\bar{Z}_{1}}{\bar{Z}_{2}}$ where $\mathrm{Z}_{2} \neq 0$
(vii) $\overline{\mathrm{z}^{\mathrm{n}}}=(\overline{\mathrm{z}})^{\mathrm{n}}$

\section*{Algebra of complex numbers:}

\section*{(i) Addition of two complex numbers:}

Let $\mathrm{z}_{1}=\mathrm{a}+\mathrm{ib}$ and $\mathrm{z}_{2}=\mathrm{c}+\mathrm{id}$ be any two complex numbers then their sum is defined as
$\mathrm{z}_{1}+\mathrm{z}_{2}=\mathrm{a}+\mathrm{ib}+\mathrm{c}+\mathrm{id}=(\mathrm{a}+\mathrm{c})+\mathrm{i}(\mathrm{b}+\mathrm{d}) \in \mathrm{C}$
$\mathrm{z}+\overline{\mathrm{z}}=2 \mathrm{a} \quad$ Real number.

\section*{(ii) Difference of two complex numbers:}

Let $\mathrm{z}_{1}=\mathrm{a}+\mathrm{ib}$ and $\mathrm{z}_{2}=\mathrm{c}+\mathrm{id}$ be any two complex numbers then their difference is defined as
$\mathrm{z}_{1}-\mathrm{z}_{2}=(\mathrm{a}+\mathrm{ib})-(\mathrm{c}+\mathrm{id})=(\mathrm{a}-\mathrm{c})+\mathrm{i}(\mathrm{b}-\mathrm{d}) \in \mathrm{C}$
$\mathrm{z}-\overline{\mathrm{Z}}=2 \mathrm{ib} \quad$ Imaginary number.

\section*{(iii) Multiplication of two compelx numbers:}

Let $\mathrm{z}_{1}=\mathrm{a}+\mathrm{ib}$ and $\mathrm{z}_{2}=\mathrm{c}+\mathrm{id}$ be any two complex numbers then their product is defined as,
$$\begin{aligned}
\mathrm{z}_{1} \mathrm{z}_{2} & =(\mathrm{a}+\mathrm{ib})(\mathrm{c}+\mathrm{id}) \\
& =\mathrm{ac}+\mathrm{i} a d+\mathrm{i} b c+\mathrm{i}^{2} \mathrm{bd} \\
& =(\mathrm{ac}-\mathrm{bd})+\mathrm{i}(\mathrm{ad}+\mathrm{bc}) \in \mathrm{C}
\end{aligned}$$
$\mathrm{z} \overline{\mathrm{Z}}=(\mathrm{a}+\mathrm{ib})(\mathrm{a}-\mathrm{ib})=\mathrm{a}^{2}+\mathrm{b}^{2}$

\section*{(iv) Division of two complex numbers:}

Let $\mathrm{z}_{1}=\mathrm{a}+\mathrm{ib}$ and $\mathrm{z}_{2}=\mathrm{c}+\mathrm{id} \neq 0$ be any two complex numbers then their quotient is defined as
$$\frac{\mathrm{z}_{1}}{\mathrm{z}_{2}}=\frac{\mathrm{a}+\mathrm{ib}}{\mathrm{c}+\mathrm{id}} \times \frac{\mathrm{c}-\mathrm{id}}{\mathrm{c}-\mathrm{id}}=\left[\frac{\mathrm{ac}+\mathrm{bd}}{\mathrm{c}^{2}+\mathrm{d}^{2}}\right]+\mathrm{i}\left[\frac{\mathrm{bc}-\mathrm{ad}}{\mathrm{c}^{2}+\mathrm{d}^{2}}\right]$$

\section*{Modulus of a complex number:}

If $\mathrm{z}=\mathrm{a}+\mathrm{ib}$ is a complex number then the modulus (or) absolute value of z is defined as $\sqrt{\mathrm{a}^{2}+\mathrm{b}^{2}}$ and is denoted by $|z|$. Thus, if $z=a+i b$ then $|z|=\sqrt{a^{2}+b^{2}}$.

\section*{Note:}
(i) $|\bar{z}|=|z|=\sqrt{a^{2}+b^{2}}$
(ii) $|\mathrm{z}|=\sqrt{\mathrm{Z} \overline{\mathrm{Z}}}=\sqrt{\mathrm{a}^{2}+\mathrm{b}^{2}}$
(iii) $\operatorname{Re}(z) \leq|z|$ and $\operatorname{Im}(z) \leq|z|$

\section*{Polar form of a Complex Number:}

Let $(r, \theta)$ be the Polar co-ordinates of the point $P$ representing the complex number $\mathrm{z}=\mathrm{a}+\mathrm{ib}$. Then from the fig. we get,
$$\begin{aligned}
& \cos \theta=\frac{O M}{O P}=\frac{a}{r} \text { and } \sin \theta=\frac{P M}{O P}=\frac{b}{r} \\
& \Rightarrow a=r \cos \theta \text { and } b=r \sin \theta
\end{aligned}$$
where $r=\sqrt{a^{2}+b^{2}}=|a+i b|$ is called the modulus of $z=a+i b$.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_07_b62b456faa89cfbc166dg-036.jpg?height=375&width=390&top_left_y=233&top_left_x=1331)

Also, $\tan \theta=\frac{b}{a} \Rightarrow \theta=\tan ^{-1}\left(\frac{b}{a}\right)$ is called the amplitude or argument of $z=a+i b$ and denoted by $\operatorname{amp}(z) \operatorname{or} \arg (z)$ and is measured as the angle in positive sense. Thus, $\arg (z)=\theta=\tan ^{-1}\left(\frac{b}{a}\right)$.

Hence $\mathrm{z}=\mathrm{a}+\mathrm{ib}=\mathrm{r}(\cos \theta+\mathrm{i} \sin \theta)$ is called the Polar form or the modulus amplitude form of the complex number.

\section*{Theorems of Complex numbers:}
1) The product of two complex numbers is a complex number whose modulus is the product of their modulii and whose amplitude is the sum of their amplitudes
$$\text { i.e, }\left|z_{1} z_{2}\right|=\left|z_{1}\right|\left|z_{2}\right|$$
and $\arg \left(z_{1} z_{2}\right)=\arg \left(z_{1}\right)+\arg \left(z_{2}\right)$
2) The quotient of two complex numbers is a complex number whose modulus is the quotient of their modulii and whose amplitude is the difference of their amplitudes.
$$\text { i.e }\left|\frac{z_{1}}{z_{2}}\right|=\frac{\left|z_{1}\right|}{\left|z_{2}\right|} \text { wehre } Z_{2} \neq 0 \text { and } \arg \left(\frac{z_{1}}{z_{2}}\right)=\arg \left(z_{1}\right)-\arg \left(z_{2}\right) .$$

\section*{Euler's formula:}

The symbol $\mathrm{e}^{\mathrm{i} \theta}$ is defined by $\mathrm{e}^{\mathrm{i} \theta}=\cos \theta+\mathrm{i} \sin \theta$ is known as Euler's formula.
If $\mathrm{z} \neq 0$ then $\mathrm{z}=\mathrm{r}(\cos \theta+\mathrm{i} \sin \theta)=\mathrm{re}^{\mathrm{i} \theta}$. This is called the exponential form of the complex number z .
Note: If $\mathrm{z}=\mathrm{re}^{\mathrm{i} \theta}$ then $\overline{\mathrm{z}}=\mathrm{re}^{-\mathrm{i} \theta}$.

\section*{Multiplication and Division of complex numbers (Geometrical proof not needed)}

Let $\mathrm{z}_{1}=\mathrm{r}_{1}\left(\cos \theta_{1}+\mathrm{i} \sin \theta_{1}\right)$ and $\mathrm{z}_{2}=\mathrm{r}_{2}\left(\cos \theta_{2}+\mathrm{i} \sin \theta_{2}\right)$
be any two complex numbers in Polar form then their product is given by
$$z_{1} z_{2}=r_{1} r_{2}\left[\cos \left(\theta_{1}+\theta_{2}\right)+i \sin \left(\theta_{1}+\theta_{2}\right)\right]$$

Also the division of the above two complex numbers is given by
$$\frac{z_{1}}{z_{2}}=\frac{r_{1}}{r_{2}}\left[\cos \left(\theta_{1}-\theta_{2}\right)+i \sin \left(\theta_{1}-\theta_{2}\right)\right] \text { where } z_{2} \neq 0 \text {. }$$

\section*{WORKED EXAMPLES}

\section*{PART - A}
1. If $\mathrm{z}_{1}=2+3 i$ and $\mathrm{z}_{2}=4-5 i$ find $\mathrm{z}_{1}+\mathrm{z}_{2}$.

\section*{Solution:}
$$\begin{aligned}
\text { Given: } \quad \begin{aligned}
\mathrm{z}_{1} & =2+3 \mathrm{i} \quad \& \mathrm{z}_{2}=4-5 \mathrm{i} \\
\mathrm{z}_{1}+\mathrm{z}_{2} & =(2+3 \mathrm{i})+(4-5 \mathrm{i}) \\
& =2+3 \mathrm{i}+4-5 \mathrm{i} \\
& =(2+4)+(3 \mathrm{i}-5 \mathrm{i}) \\
\Rightarrow \mathrm{z}_{1}+\mathrm{z}_{2} & =6-2 \mathrm{i}
\end{aligned}
\end{aligned}$$
2. If $z_{1}=3-4 i$ and $z_{2}=-2+3 i$ find the value of $2 z_{1}-3 z_{2}$.

\section*{Solution:}
$$\begin{aligned}
\text { Given: } \mathrm{z}_{1}= & 3-4 \mathrm{i} \& \mathrm{z}_{2}=-2+3 \mathrm{i} \\
2 \mathrm{z}_{1}-3 \mathrm{z}_{2} \quad & =2(3-4 \mathrm{i})-3(-2+3 \mathrm{i}) \\
& =6-8 \mathrm{i}+6-9 \mathrm{i} \\
\Rightarrow 2 \mathrm{z}_{1}-3 \mathrm{z}_{2} & =12-17 \mathrm{i}
\end{aligned}$$
3. Express: $(3+2 \mathrm{i})(4+2 \mathrm{i})$ in $\mathrm{a}+\mathrm{ib}$ form.

\section*{Solution:}
$$\begin{aligned}
(3+2 \mathrm{i})(4+2 \mathrm{i}) & =12+6 \mathrm{i}+8 \mathrm{i}+4 \mathrm{i}^{2} \\
& =12+14 \mathrm{i}-4 \\
& =8+14 \mathrm{i}=\mathrm{a}+\mathrm{ib} \text { form. }
\end{aligned}$$
4. Find the real and imaginary parts of $\frac{1}{3+2 \mathrm{i}}$.

\section*{Solution:}

Let $\mathrm{z}=\frac{1}{3+2 \mathrm{i}}=\frac{1}{3+2 \mathrm{i}} \times \frac{3-2 \mathrm{i}}{3-2 \mathrm{i}}$
$$\begin{aligned}
&=\frac{3-2 \mathrm{i}}{(3)^{2}-(2 \mathrm{i})^{2}} \\
&=\frac{3-2 \mathrm{i}}{9+4} \\
&=\frac{3-2 \mathrm{i}}{13} \\
& \Rightarrow \mathrm{z}=\frac{3}{13}-\frac{2 \mathrm{i}}{13} \\
& \therefore \operatorname{Re}(\mathrm{z})=\frac{3}{13} \& \operatorname{Im}(\mathrm{z})=\frac{-2}{13}
\end{aligned}$$
5. Find the conjugate of $\frac{1}{1+\mathrm{i}}$.

\section*{Solution:}
$$\begin{aligned}
& \text { Let } \mathrm{z}=\frac{1}{1+\mathrm{i}}=\frac{1}{1+\mathrm{i}} \times \frac{1-\mathrm{i}}{1-\mathrm{i}} \\
&=\frac{1-\mathrm{i}}{(1)^{2}-(\mathrm{i})^{2}} \\
&=\frac{1-\mathrm{i}}{1+1} \\
&=\frac{1-\mathrm{i}}{2} \\
& \Rightarrow \mathrm{z}=\frac{1}{2}-\frac{\mathrm{i}}{2} \\
& \therefore \text { Conjugate }: \overline{\mathrm{z}}=\frac{1}{2}+\frac{\mathrm{i}}{2}
\end{aligned}$$
6. Find the modulus and amplitude of $1+\mathrm{i}$.

\section*{Solution:}

Let $\mathrm{z}=1+\mathrm{i}$
Here $\mathrm{a}=1 \quad \& \mathrm{~b}=1$
$$\begin{aligned}
\text { Modulus }: \mid \mathrm{z} & =\sqrt{\mathrm{a}^{2}+\mathrm{b}^{2}}=\sqrt{(1)^{2}+(1)^{2}}=\sqrt{1+1}=\sqrt{2} \\
\text { and } \operatorname{amp}(\mathrm{z}) & =\theta=\tan ^{-1}\left(\frac{\mathrm{~b}}{\mathrm{a}}\right)=\tan ^{-1}\left(\frac{1}{1}\right)=\tan ^{-1}(1) \\
\Rightarrow \theta & =45^{\circ}
\end{aligned}$$

\section*{PART -B}
1. Find the real and imaginary parts of $\frac{4+5 i}{3-2 i}$.

\section*{Solution:}
$$\begin{aligned}
\text { Let } \begin{aligned}
\mathrm{z} & =\frac{4+5 \mathrm{i}}{3-2 \mathrm{i}}=\frac{4+5 \mathrm{i}}{3-2 \mathrm{i}} \times \frac{3+2 \mathrm{i}}{3+2 \mathrm{i}} \\
& =\frac{12+8 \mathrm{i}+15 \mathrm{i}+10 \mathrm{i}^{2}}{(3)^{2}-(2 \mathrm{i})^{2}} \\
& =\frac{12+23 \mathrm{i}-10}{9+4} \\
& =\frac{2+23 \mathrm{i}}{13} \\
\mathrm{z} & =\frac{2}{13}+\frac{23 \mathrm{i}}{13} \\
\therefore \operatorname{Re}(\mathrm{z}) & =\frac{2}{13} \quad \& \operatorname{Im}(\mathrm{z})=\frac{23}{13}
\end{aligned} . \begin{aligned}
\end{aligned}{ }^{2}
\end{aligned}$$
2. Express the complex number $\frac{1}{3-2 \mathrm{i}}+\frac{1}{2-3 \mathrm{i}}$ in $\mathrm{a}+\mathrm{ib}$ form.

\section*{Solution:}
$$\begin{aligned}
& \text { Let } \mathrm{z}=\frac{1}{3-2 \mathrm{i}}+\frac{1}{2-3 \mathrm{i}} \\
& =\frac{1}{3-2 \mathrm{i}} \times \frac{3+2 \mathrm{i}}{3+2 \mathrm{i}}+\frac{1}{2-3 \mathrm{i}}+\frac{2+3 \mathrm{i}}{2+3 \mathrm{i}} \\
& =\frac{3+2 \mathrm{i}}{3^{2}+2^{2}}+\frac{2+3 \mathrm{i}}{2^{2}+3^{2}} \\
& =\frac{3+2 i+2+3 i}{13} \\
& =\frac{5+5 \mathrm{i}}{13} \\
& \mathrm{z}=\frac{5}{13}+\frac{5}{13} \mathrm{i}=\mathrm{a}+\mathrm{ib} \text { form }
\end{aligned}$$
3. Find the modulus and argument of the complex number $\frac{1-\mathrm{i}}{1+\mathrm{i}}$.

\section*{Solution:}

Let $\mathrm{z}=\frac{1-\mathrm{i}}{1+\mathrm{i}}=\frac{1-\mathrm{i}}{1+\mathrm{i}} \times \frac{1-\mathrm{i}}{1-\mathrm{i}}$
$$\begin{aligned}
& =\frac{1-\mathrm{i}-\mathrm{i}+\mathrm{i}^{2}}{(1)^{2}-(\mathrm{i})^{2}} \\
& =\frac{1-2 \mathrm{i}-1}{1+1} \\
& =\frac{-2 \mathrm{i}}{2}
\end{aligned}$$
$$\mathrm{z}=-\mathrm{i} \text { where } \mathrm{a}=0 \& \mathrm{~b}=-1$$

Modulus : $|z|=\sqrt{\mathrm{a}^{2}+\mathrm{b}^{2}}=\sqrt{(0)^{2}+(-1)^{2}}=\sqrt{1}=1$
Argument : $\tan \theta=\frac{\mathrm{b}}{\mathrm{a}}=\frac{-1}{0}=\infty$
$$\theta=\tan ^{-1}(\infty)=90^{\circ}$$

The complex number $-\mathrm{i}=(0,-1)$ lies IIIrd Quadrant.
Hence amplitude $=180^{\circ}+90^{\circ}=270^{\circ}$.

\section*{PART - C}
1. Find the real and imaginary parts of the complex number $\frac{(1+\mathrm{i})(2-\mathrm{i})}{1+3 \mathrm{i}}$.

\section*{Solution:}
$$\begin{aligned}
& \text { Let } \mathrm{z}=\frac{(1+\mathrm{i})(2-\mathrm{i})}{1+3 \mathrm{i}} \\
& =\frac{2-\mathrm{i}+2 \mathrm{i}-\mathrm{i}^{2}}{1+3 \mathrm{i}} \\
& =\frac{2+\mathrm{i}+1}{1+3 \mathrm{i}}
\end{aligned}$$
$$\begin{aligned}
& =\frac{3+\mathrm{i}}{1+3 \mathrm{i}} \times \frac{1-3 \mathrm{i}}{1-3 \mathrm{i}} \\
& =\frac{3-9 \mathrm{i}+\mathrm{i}-3 \mathrm{i}^{2}}{(1)^{2}-(3 \mathrm{i})^{2}} \\
& =\frac{3-8 \mathrm{i}+3}{1+9} \\
& =\frac{6-8 \mathrm{i}}{10} \\
& =\frac{3-4 \mathrm{i}}{5} \\
& z=\frac{3}{5}-\frac{4 \mathrm{i}}{5}=\mathrm{a}+\mathrm{ib} \text { form. } \\
\therefore \operatorname{Re}(\mathrm{z}) & =\frac{3}{5} \& \operatorname{Im}(\mathrm{z})=-\frac{4}{5}
\end{aligned}$$
2. Express the complex number $\frac{i-4}{3-2 i}+\frac{4 i+1}{2-3 i}$ in $a+i b$ form.

\section*{Solution:}
$$\text { Let } \begin{aligned}
z & =\frac{i-4}{3-2 i}+\frac{4 i+1}{2-3 i} \\
& =\frac{i-4}{3-2 i} \times \frac{3+2 i}{3+2 i}+\frac{4 i+1}{2-3 i} \times \frac{2+3 i}{2+3 i} \\
& =\frac{3 i-12+2 i^{2}-8 i}{3^{2}+2^{2}}+\frac{8 i+2+12 i^{2}+3 i}{2^{2}+3^{2}} \\
& =\frac{-5 i-14}{13}+\frac{11 i-10}{13} \\
& =\frac{6 i-24}{13} \\
& =\frac{-24+6 i}{13}=\frac{-24}{13}+\frac{6 i}{13}=a+i b \text { form }
\end{aligned}$$
3. Find the modulus and amplitude of $\frac{1+3 \sqrt{3} \mathrm{i}}{\sqrt{3}+2 \mathrm{i}}$.

\section*{Solution:}
$$\text { Let } \begin{aligned}
\mathrm{z} & =\frac{\overline{1}+3 \sqrt{3} \mathrm{i}}{\sqrt{3}+2 \mathrm{i}} \\
& =\frac{1+3 \sqrt{3} \mathrm{i}}{\sqrt{3}+2 \mathrm{i}} \times \frac{\sqrt{3}-2 \mathrm{i}}{\sqrt{3}-2 \mathrm{i}} \\
& =\frac{\sqrt{3}-2 \mathrm{i}+9 \mathrm{i}-6 \sqrt{3} \mathrm{i}^{2}}{(\sqrt{3})^{2}-(2 \mathrm{i})^{2}} \\
& =\frac{\sqrt{3}+7 \mathrm{i}+6 \sqrt{3}}{3+4} \\
& =\frac{7 \sqrt{3}+7 \mathrm{i}}{7} \\
& =\frac{7(\sqrt{3}+\mathrm{i})}{7} \\
\mathrm{z} & =\sqrt{3}+\mathrm{i}=\mathrm{a}+\mathrm{ib} \text { form }
\end{aligned}$$

Here $a=\sqrt{3} \quad \& \quad b=1$
$\therefore$ Modulus: $|\mathrm{z}|=\sqrt{\mathrm{a}^{2}+\mathrm{b}^{2}}=\sqrt{(3)^{2}+(1)^{2}}=\sqrt{3+\sqrt{1}}=4=2$
Amplitude : $\theta=\tan ^{-1}\left(\frac{\mathrm{~b}}{\mathrm{a}}\right)=\tan ^{-1}\left(\frac{1}{\sqrt{3}}\right)=30^{\circ}$
4. Find the modulus and argument of the complex number $\frac{5-\mathrm{i}}{2-3 \mathrm{i}}$.

\section*{Solution:}
$$\text { Let } \begin{aligned}
\mathrm{z} & =\frac{5-\mathrm{i}}{2-3 \mathrm{i}} \\
& =\frac{5-\mathrm{i}}{2-3 \mathrm{i}} \times \frac{2+3 \mathrm{i}}{2+3 \mathrm{i}} \\
& =\frac{10+15 \mathrm{i}-2 \mathrm{i}-3 \mathrm{i}^{2}}{(2)^{2}-\left(3 \mathrm{i}^{2}\right.} \\
& =\frac{10+13 \mathrm{i}+3}{4+9} \\
& =\frac{13+13 \mathrm{i}}{13} \\
& =\frac{13(1+\mathrm{i})}{13}
\end{aligned}$$
$\mathrm{z}=1+\mathrm{i}=\mathrm{a}+\mathrm{ib}$ form
Here $\mathrm{a}=1 \& \mathrm{~b}=1$
Modulus : $|\mathrm{z}|=\sqrt{\mathrm{a}^{2}+\mathrm{b}^{2}}=\sqrt{(1)^{2}+(1)^{2}}=\sqrt{1+1}=\sqrt{2}$
Amplitude : $\theta=\tan ^{-1}\left(\frac{\mathrm{~b}}{\mathrm{a}}\right)=\tan ^{-1}\left(\frac{1}{1}\right)=\tan ^{-1}(1)$
$\Rightarrow \theta=45^{\circ}$

\section*{Argand Diagram}
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_07_b62b456faa89cfbc166dg-041.jpg?height=372&width=390&top_left_y=1257&top_left_x=1339)

Every complex number $\mathrm{a}+\mathrm{ib}$ can be considered as an ordered pair $(\mathrm{a}, \mathrm{b})$ of real numbers, we can represent such number by a point in xy-plane called the complex plane and such a representation is also known as the argand diagram. The complex number $\mathrm{z}=\mathrm{a}+\mathrm{ib}$ represented by $\mathrm{P}(\mathrm{z})$ then the distance between z and the origin is the modulus. i.e $|\mathrm{z}|=\sqrt{\mathrm{a}^{2}+\mathrm{b}^{2}}$

Here the set of real numbers $(x, 0)$ corresponds to the x -axis called real axis and the set of Imaginary numbers $(0, y)$ correponds to the y -axis called the imaginary axis.

\section*{Result:}

The distance between the two complex numbers $z_{1}$ and $z_{2}$ is $\left|z_{1}-z_{2}\right|$. Thus, if $z_{1}=x_{1}+i y_{1}$ and $\mathrm{z}_{2}=\mathrm{x}_{2}+\mathrm{iy} \mathrm{y}_{2}$ then $\left|\mathrm{z}_{1}-\mathrm{z}_{2}\right|=\sqrt{\left(\mathrm{x}_{1}-\mathrm{x}_{2}\right)^{2}+\left(\mathrm{y}_{1}-\mathrm{y}_{2}\right)^{2}}$.

\section*{Collinear Points:}

If $A, B$ and $C$ are any three points representing the complex numbers $x_{1}+i y_{1}, x_{2}+i y_{2}$ and $x_{3}+i y_{3}$ respectively, are collinear then the required condition is, the area of $\triangle \mathrm{ABC}$ is zero.
i.e. $\frac{1}{2}\left[x_{1}\left(y_{2}-y_{3}\right)+x_{2}\left(y_{3}-y_{1}\right)+x_{3}\left(y_{1}-y_{2}\right)\right]=0$
i.e $x_{1}\left(y_{2}-y_{3}\right)+x_{2}\left(y_{3}-y_{1}\right)+x_{3}\left(y_{1}-y_{2}\right)=0$
i.e. $\left|\begin{array}{lll}x_{1} & y_{1} & 1 \\ x_{2} & y_{2} & 1 \\ x_{3} & y_{3} & 1\end{array}\right|=0$

\section*{Condition for square:}
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_07_b62b456faa89cfbc166dg-042.jpg?height=341&width=348&top_left_y=157&top_left_x=1360)

If $\mathrm{A}, \mathrm{B}, \mathrm{C}$ and D are any four complex numbers representing the vertices of a square then the required condition is
(i) $\mathrm{AB}=\mathrm{BC}=\mathrm{CD}=\mathrm{DA}$
i.e four sides are equal.
(ii) $\mathrm{AC}=\mathrm{BD}$
i.e the diagonals are equal
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_07_b62b456faa89cfbc166dg-042.jpg?height=263&width=281&top_left_y=616&top_left_x=1352)

\section*{Condition for rectangle}

If $\mathrm{A}, \mathrm{B}, \mathrm{C}$ and D are any four complex numbers represents the vertices of a rectangle then the required condition is,
(i) $\mathrm{AB}=\mathrm{CD}$ and $\mathrm{BC}=\mathrm{DA}$.
i.e Opposite sides are equal.
(ii) $\mathrm{AC}=\mathrm{BD}$
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_07_b62b456faa89cfbc166dg-042.jpg?height=271&width=398&top_left_y=1027&top_left_x=1275)
i.e the diagonals are equal.

\section*{Condition for rhombus}

If $\mathrm{A}, \mathrm{B}, \mathrm{C}$ and D are any four complex numbers represents the vertices of a rhombus then the required condition is
$\mathrm{AB}=\mathrm{BC}=\mathrm{CD}=\mathrm{DA}$
i.e four sides are equal.

\section*{Condition for Parallelogram}

If $\mathrm{A}, \mathrm{B}, \mathrm{C}$ and D are any four complex numbers represents the vertices of a parallelogram then the required condition is either,
mid-point of the diagonal $\mathrm{AC}=$ mid.point of the diagonal BD .
(or) The length of the opposite sides are equal. i.e $\mathrm{AB}=\mathrm{CD} \& \mathrm{BC}=\mathrm{DA}$.

\section*{Note:}
(i) To find length of the sides and diagonals of square, rectangle, rhombus and parallelogram apply the distance formula.
i.e distance: $\mathrm{AB}=\sqrt{\left(\mathrm{x}_{1}-\mathrm{x}_{2}\right)^{2}+\left(\mathrm{y}_{1}-\mathrm{y}_{2}\right)^{2}}$ etc.
(ii) To find the mid.point of the diagonals of the parallelogram apply middle point formula, $(x, y)=\left[\frac{x_{1}+x_{2}}{2}, \frac{y_{1}+y_{2}}{2}\right]$

\section*{WORKED EXAMPLES}

\section*{PART - A}
1. Find the distance between the complex numbers $2+\mathrm{i}$ and $1-2 \mathrm{i}$.

\section*{Solution:}

Let $\mathrm{z}_{1}=2+\mathrm{i} \& \mathrm{z}_{2}=1-2 \mathrm{i}$
$\therefore$ Distance: $\mathrm{z}_{1} \mathrm{z}_{2}=\sqrt{(2-1)^{2}+(1+2)^{2}}$
$$\begin{aligned}
& =\sqrt{(1)^{2}+(3)^{2}} \\
& =\sqrt{1+9} \\
\Rightarrow z_{1} z_{2} & =\sqrt{10}
\end{aligned}$$

\section*{PART - B}
1. Show that the complex numbers $1+2 \mathrm{i}, 3-2 \mathrm{i}$ and $6-8 \mathrm{i}$ are collinear.

\section*{Solution:}

Given complex numbers are
A : $1+2 \mathrm{i}=(1,2)$
B: $3-2 \mathrm{i}=(3,-2)$
\& C : $6-8 \mathrm{i}=(6,-8)$
Condition for collinear is $\left|\begin{array}{lll}x_{1} & y_{1} & 1 \\ x_{2} & y_{2} & 1 \\ x_{3} & y_{3} & 1\end{array}\right|=0$
LHS: $\left|\begin{array}{ccc}1 & 2 & 1 \\ 3 & -2 & 1 \\ 6 & -8 & 1\end{array}\right|=1\left|\begin{array}{ll}-2 & 1 \\ -8 & 1\end{array}\right|-2\left|\begin{array}{ll}3 & 1 \\ 6 & 1\end{array}\right|+1\left|\begin{array}{cc}3 & -2 \\ 6 & -8\end{array}\right|$
$$\begin{aligned}
& =1(-2+8)-2(3-6)+1(-24+12) \\
& =1(6)-2(-3)+1(-12) \\
& =6+6-12 \\
& =12-12 \\
& =0
\end{aligned}$$
$\therefore$ The given complex numebrs are collinear.
2. Show that the complex numbers $-1+\mathrm{i}, 3+2 \mathrm{i}, 2+2 \mathrm{i}$ and $-2+\mathrm{i}$ form a parallelogram.

\section*{Solution:}

Let the given complex numbers are
A : $-1+\mathrm{i}=(-1,1)$
B: $3+2 \mathrm{i}=(3,2)$
C : $2+2 \mathrm{i}=(2,2)$
D : $-2+\mathrm{i}=(-2,1)$

Mid-point of $\mathrm{AC}=\left[\frac{\mathrm{x}_{1}+\mathrm{x}_{2}}{2}, \frac{\mathrm{y}_{1}+\mathrm{y}_{2}}{2}\right]=\left[\frac{-1+2}{2}, \frac{1+2}{2}\right]=\left[\frac{1}{2}, \frac{3}{2}\right]$ $\qquad$
Mid-point of $\mathrm{BD}=\left[\frac{3-2}{2}, \frac{2+1}{2}\right]=\left[\frac{1}{2}, \frac{3}{2}\right]$ $\qquad$
From (1) \& (2), Mid-point of AC = Mid-point of BD.
$\Rightarrow$ Given complex numbers form a parallelogram.

\section*{PART - C}
1. Prove that the points representing the complex numbers $3+2 \mathrm{i}, 5+4 \mathrm{i}, 3+6 \mathrm{i}$ and $1+4 \mathrm{i}$ form a square.

\section*{Solution:}

Let the given complex number be
$\mathrm{A}: 3+2 \mathrm{i}=(3,2)$
B : $5+4 \mathrm{i}=(5,4)$
$\mathrm{C}: 3+6 \mathrm{i}=(3,6)$
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_07_b62b456faa89cfbc166dg-044.jpg?height=310&width=333&top_left_y=712&top_left_x=1357)
\& D : $1+4 \mathrm{i}=(1,4)$
Now,
$A B=\sqrt{\left(x_{1}-x_{2}\right)^{2}+\left(y_{1}-y_{2}\right)^{2}}=\sqrt{(3-5)^{2}+(2-4)^{2}}=\sqrt{(-2)^{2}+(-2)^{2}}=\sqrt{4+4}=\sqrt{8}$
$\mathrm{BC}=\sqrt{\left(\mathrm{x}_{1}-\mathrm{x}_{2}\right)^{2}+\left(\mathrm{y}_{1}-\mathrm{y}_{2}\right)^{2}}=\sqrt{(5-3)^{2}+(4-6)^{2}}=\sqrt{(2)^{2}+(-2)^{2}}=\sqrt{4+4}=\sqrt{8}$
$\mathrm{CD}=\sqrt{\left(\mathrm{x}_{1}-\mathrm{x}_{2}\right)^{2}+\left(\mathrm{y}_{1}-\mathrm{y}_{2}\right)^{2}}=\sqrt{(3-1)^{2}+(6-4)^{2}}=\sqrt{(2)^{2}+(2)^{2}}=\sqrt{4+4}=\sqrt{8}$
and DA $=\sqrt{\left(\mathrm{x}_{1}-\mathrm{x}_{2}\right)^{2}+\left(\mathrm{y}_{1}-\mathrm{y}_{2}\right)^{2}}=\sqrt{(1-3)^{2}+(4-2)^{2}}=\sqrt{(-2)^{2}+(2)^{2}}=\sqrt{4+4}=\sqrt{8}$
Also, $\mathrm{AC}=\sqrt{\left(\mathrm{x}_{1}-\mathrm{x}_{2}\right)^{2}+\left(\mathrm{y}_{1}-\mathrm{y}_{2}\right)^{2}}=\sqrt{(3-3)^{2}+(2-6)^{2}}=\sqrt{(0)^{2}+(-4)^{2}}=\sqrt{16}=4$
$\mathrm{BD}=\sqrt{\left(\mathrm{x}_{1}-\mathrm{x}_{2}\right)^{2}+\left(\mathrm{y}_{1}-\mathrm{y}_{2}\right)^{2}}=\sqrt{(5-1)^{2}+(4-4)^{2}}=\sqrt{(4)^{2}+(0)^{2}}=\sqrt{16}=4$
Here the sides $\mathrm{AB}=\mathrm{BC}=\mathrm{CD}=\mathrm{DA}=\sqrt{8}$
\& diagonals $\mathrm{AC}=\mathrm{BD}=4$
$\therefore$ The given complex numbers form a square.
2. Prove that the points representing the complex numbers $2-2 i, 8+4 i, 5+7 i$ and $-1+i$ form a rectangle.

\section*{Solution:}

Let the given complex numbers be,
A: $2-2 \mathrm{i}=(2,-2)$
B : $8+4 \mathrm{i}=(8,4)$
C: $5+7 \mathrm{i}=(5,7)$
\& $\mathrm{D}:-1+\mathrm{i}=(-1,1)$
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_07_b62b456faa89cfbc166dg-044.jpg?height=300&width=432&top_left_y=2041&top_left_x=1227)

Now, $A B=\sqrt{\left(x_{1}-x_{2}\right)^{2}+\left(y_{1}-y_{2}\right)^{2}}=\sqrt{(2-8)^{2}+(-2-4)^{2}}=\sqrt{(-6)^{2}+(-6)^{2}}=\sqrt{36+36}=\sqrt{72}$
Now, $B C=\sqrt{\left(x_{1}-x_{2}\right)^{2}+\left(y_{1}-y_{2}\right)^{2}}=\sqrt{(8-5)^{2}+(4-7)^{2}}=\sqrt{(3)^{2}+(-3)^{2}}=\sqrt{9+9}=\sqrt{18}$
$C D=\sqrt{\left(x_{1}-x_{2}\right)^{2}+\left(y_{1}-y_{2}\right)^{2}}=\sqrt{(5+1)^{2}+(7-1)^{2}}=\sqrt{(6)^{2}+(6)^{2}}=\sqrt{36+36}=\sqrt{72}$
\& $D A=\sqrt{\left(x_{1}-x_{2}\right)^{2}+\left(y_{1}-y_{2}\right)^{2}}=\sqrt{(-1-2)^{2}+(1+2)^{2}}=\sqrt{(-3)^{2}+(3)^{2}}=\sqrt{9+9}=\sqrt{18}$
Also, $\mathrm{AC}=\sqrt{\left(\mathrm{x}_{1}-\mathrm{x}_{2}\right)^{2}+\left(\mathrm{y}_{1}-\mathrm{y}_{2}\right)^{2}}=\sqrt{(2-5)^{2}+(-2-7)^{2}}=\sqrt{(-3)^{2}+(-9)^{2}}=\sqrt{9+81}=\sqrt{90}$
$\mathrm{BD}=\sqrt{\left(\mathrm{x}_{1}-\mathrm{x}_{2}\right)^{2}+\left(\mathrm{y}_{1}-\mathrm{y}_{2}\right)^{2}}=\sqrt{(8+1)^{2}+(4-1)^{2}}=\sqrt{(9)^{2}+(3)^{2}}=\sqrt{81+9}=\sqrt{90}$
Here the sides $\mathrm{AB}=\mathrm{CD}=\sqrt{72}$
$$\mathrm{BC}=\mathrm{DA}=\sqrt{18}$$
and diagonals $\mathrm{AC}=\mathrm{BD}=\sqrt{90}$
$\therefore$ The given complex numbers form a rectangle.
3. Prove that the points $3+4 \mathrm{i}, 9+8 \mathrm{i}, 5+2 \mathrm{i}$ and $-1-2 \mathrm{i}$ form a rhombus in the argand diagram.

\section*{Solution:}

Let the given complex numbers be,
$\mathrm{A}: 3+4 \mathrm{i}=(3,4)$
B : $9+8 \mathrm{i}=(9,8)$
C: $5+2 \mathrm{i}=(5,2)$
\& $\mathrm{D}:-1-2 \mathrm{i}=(-1,-2)$
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_07_b62b456faa89cfbc166dg-045.jpg?height=318&width=497&top_left_y=1123&top_left_x=1179)

B $(9,8)$
$A B=\sqrt{\left(x_{1}-x_{2}\right)^{2}+\left(y_{1}-y_{2}\right)^{2}}=\sqrt{(3-9)^{2}+(4-8)^{2}}=\sqrt{(-6)^{2}+(-4)^{2}}=\sqrt{36+16}=\sqrt{52}$
$B C=\sqrt{\left(x_{1}-x_{2}\right)^{2}+\left(y_{1}-y_{2}\right)^{2}}=\sqrt{(9-5)^{2}+(8-2)^{2}}=\sqrt{(4)^{2}+(6)^{2}}=\sqrt{16+36}=\sqrt{52}$
$C D=\sqrt{\left(x_{1}-x_{2}\right)^{2}+\left(y_{1}-y_{2}\right)^{2}}=\sqrt{(5+1)^{2}+(2+2)^{2}}=\sqrt{(6)^{2}+(4)^{2}}=\sqrt{36+16}=\sqrt{52}$
\& $D A=\sqrt{\left(x_{1}-x_{2}\right)^{2}+\left(y_{1}-y_{2}\right)^{2}}=\sqrt{(-1-3)^{2}+(-2-4)^{2}}=\sqrt{(-4)^{2}+(-6)^{2}}=\sqrt{16+36}=\sqrt{52}$
Here the sides, $\mathrm{AB}=\mathrm{BC}=\mathrm{CD}=\mathrm{DA}=\sqrt{52}$.
$\therefore$ The given complex number form a rhombus.

\subsection*{2.2 DE-MOIVRE'S THEOREM}

\section*{DeMoivre's Thoerem: (Statement only)}
(i) If ' n ' is an integer positive or negative then $(\cos \theta+\mathrm{i} \sin \theta)^{\mathrm{n}}=\cos \mathrm{n} \theta+\mathrm{i} \sin \mathrm{n} \theta$.
(ii) If ' $n$ ' is a fraction, then $\cos n \theta+\mathrm{i} \sin \mathrm{n} \theta$ is one of the values of $(\cos \theta+\mathrm{i} \sin \theta)^{\mathrm{n}}$.

\section*{Results:}
1) $(\cos \theta+i \sin \theta)^{n}=\cos n \theta+i \sin n \theta$
2) $(\cos \theta+i \sin \theta)^{-n}=(\cos n \theta-i \sin n \theta)$
3) $\frac{1}{\cos \theta+i \sin \theta}=(\cos \theta+i \sin \theta)^{-1}=\cos \theta-i \sin \theta$
4) $\frac{1}{\cos \theta-i \sin \theta}=(\cos \theta-i \sin \theta)^{-1}=\cos \theta+i \sin \theta$
5) $\sin \theta+i \cos \theta=\cos \left(\frac{\pi}{2}-\theta\right)+i \sin \left(\frac{\pi}{2}-\theta\right)$

\section*{Note:}
1) $\left(\cos \theta_{1}+i \sin \theta_{1}\right)\left(\cos \theta_{2}+i \sin \theta_{2}\right)=\cos \left(\theta_{1}+\theta_{2}\right)+i \sin \left(\theta_{1}+\theta_{2}\right)$
2) $\left(\cos \theta_{1}+i \sin \theta_{1}\right)\left(\cos \theta_{2}+i \sin \theta_{2}\right)\left(\cos \theta_{3}+i \sin \theta_{3}\right)=\cos \left(\theta_{1}+\theta_{2}+\theta_{3}\right)+i \sin \left(\theta_{1}+\theta_{2}+\theta_{3}\right)$
3) $\left(\cos \theta_{1}+i \sin \theta_{1}\right)\left(\cos \theta_{2}+i \sin \theta_{2}\right) \ldots\left(\cos \theta_{n}+i \sin \theta_{n}\right)$
$$=\cos \left(\theta_{1}+\theta_{2}+\ldots \ldots . .+\theta_{n}\right)+i \sin \left(\theta_{1}+\theta_{2}+\ldots . .+\theta_{n}\right)$$

\section*{WORKED EXAMPLES}

\section*{PART - A}
1. If $z=\cos 30^{\circ}+i \sin 30^{\circ}$ what is the value of $z^{3}$.

\section*{Solution:}
$$\begin{aligned}
\mathrm{z}^{3} & =\left[\cos 30^{\circ}+\mathrm{i} \sin 30^{\circ}\right]^{3} \\
& =\cos 90^{\circ}+\mathrm{i} \sin 90^{\circ} \\
& =0+\mathrm{i}(1)=\mathrm{i}
\end{aligned}$$
2. If $z=\cos \frac{\pi}{2}+i \sin \frac{\pi}{2}$ what is the value of $z^{8}$.

\section*{Solution:}
$$\begin{aligned}
z^{8} & =\left[\cos \frac{\pi}{2}+\mathrm{i} \sin \frac{\pi}{2}\right]^{8} \\
& =\cos 8\left(\frac{\pi}{2}\right)+\mathrm{i} \sin 8\left(\frac{\pi}{2}\right) \\
& =\cos 4 \pi+\mathrm{i} \sin 4 \pi \\
& =1+\mathrm{i}(0)=1
\end{aligned}$$
3. If $\mathrm{z}=\cos 45^{\circ}-\mathrm{i} \sin 45^{\circ}$ what is the value of $\frac{1}{\mathrm{z}}$.

\section*{Solution:}
$$\frac{1}{\mathrm{z}}=\mathrm{z}^{-1}$$
$$\begin{aligned}
& =\left[\cos 45-\mathrm{i} \sin 45^{\circ}\right]^{-1} \\
& =\cos 45^{\circ}+\mathrm{i} \sin 45^{\circ} \\
& =\frac{1}{\sqrt{2}}+\mathrm{i} \frac{1}{\sqrt{2}}
\end{aligned}$$
4. If $\frac{1}{z}=\cos 60^{\circ}+i \sin 60^{\circ}$ what is the value of $z$.

\section*{Solution:}
$$\begin{aligned}
z & =\frac{1}{\frac{1}{z}} \\
& =\frac{1}{\cos 60^{\circ}+i \sin 60^{\circ}}=\left(\cos 60^{\circ}+i \sin 60^{\circ}\right)^{-1} \\
& =\cos 60^{\circ}-\mathrm{i} \sin 60^{\circ} \\
& =\frac{1}{2}-\mathrm{i} \frac{\sqrt{3}}{2}
\end{aligned}$$
5. Find the value of $\frac{\cos 3 \theta+i \sin 3 \theta}{\cos \theta-i \sin \theta}$.

\section*{Solution:}
$$\begin{aligned}
\frac{\cos 3 \theta+\mathrm{i} \sin 3 \theta}{\cos \theta-\mathrm{i} \sin \theta} & =(\cos 3 \theta+\mathrm{i} \sin 3 \theta)(\cos \theta-\mathrm{i} \sin \theta)^{-1} \\
& =(\cos 3 \theta+\mathrm{i} \sin 3 \theta)(\cos \theta+\mathrm{i} \sin \theta) \\
& =\cos (3 \theta+\theta)+\mathrm{i} \sin (3 \theta+\theta) \\
& =\cos 4 \theta+\mathrm{i} \sin 4 \theta
\end{aligned}$$
6. Simplify: $\left(\cos 20^{\circ}+\mathrm{i} \sin 20^{\circ}\right)\left(\cos 30^{\circ}+\mathrm{i} \sin 30^{\circ}\right)\left(\cos 40^{\circ}+\mathrm{i} \sin 40^{\circ}\right)$

\section*{Solution:}
$$\begin{aligned}
& \left(\cos 20^{\circ}+\mathrm{i} \sin 20^{\circ}\right)\left(\cos 30^{\circ}+\mathrm{i} \sin 30^{\circ}\right)\left(\cos 40^{\circ}+\mathrm{i} \sin 40^{\circ}\right) \\
& =\cos \left(20^{\circ}+30^{\circ}+40^{\circ}\right)+\mathrm{i} \sin \left(20^{\circ}+30^{\circ}+40^{\circ}\right) \\
& =\cos 90^{\circ}+\mathrm{i} \sin 90^{\circ} \\
& =0+\mathrm{i}(1)=\mathrm{i}
\end{aligned}$$
7. If $x=\cos \theta+i \sin \theta$ find $x+\frac{1}{x}$.

\section*{Solution:}

Given: $\mathrm{x}=\cos \theta+\mathrm{i} \sin \theta$
$$\begin{aligned}
& \Rightarrow \quad \frac{1}{x}=\cos \theta-i \sin \theta \\
& \begin{aligned}
\therefore+\frac{1}{x} & =\cos \theta+i \sin \theta+\cos \theta-i \sin \theta \\
& =2 \cos \theta
\end{aligned}
\end{aligned}$$
8. If $x=\cos \alpha+i \sin \alpha$ and $y=\cos \beta+i \sin \beta$ find $x y$.

\section*{Solution:}
$$\begin{aligned}
x y & =(\cos \alpha+i \sin \alpha)(\cos \beta+i \sin \beta) \\
& =\cos (\alpha+\beta)+i \sin (\alpha+\beta)
\end{aligned}$$
9. If $a=\cos \alpha+i \sin \alpha$ and $b=\cos \beta+i \sin \beta$ find $\frac{a}{b}$.

\section*{Solution:}
$$\begin{aligned}
\frac{\mathrm{a}}{\mathrm{~b}} & =\mathrm{a}(\mathrm{~b})^{-1} \\
& =(\cos \alpha+i \sin \alpha)(\cos \beta+i \sin \beta)^{-1} \\
& =(\cos \alpha+i \sin \alpha)(\cos \beta-i \sin \beta) \\
& =\cos (\alpha-\beta)+i \sin (\alpha-\beta)
\end{aligned}$$
10. Find the product of $3\left(\cos \frac{\pi}{3}+\mathrm{i} \sin \frac{\pi}{3}\right)$ and $4\left(\cos \frac{\pi}{6}+\mathrm{i} \sin \frac{\pi}{6}\right)$.

\section*{Solution:}
$$\begin{aligned}
& 3\left(\cos \frac{\pi}{3}+\mathrm{i} \sin \frac{\pi}{3}\right) \times 4\left(\cos \frac{\pi}{6}+\mathrm{i} \sin \frac{\pi}{6}\right) \\
& =12\left[\cos \left(\frac{\pi}{3}+\frac{\pi}{6}\right)+\mathrm{i} \sin \left(\frac{\pi}{3}+\frac{\pi}{6}\right)\right] \\
& =12\left[\cos \frac{\pi}{2}+\mathrm{i} \sin \frac{\pi}{2}\right] \\
& =12[1+\mathrm{i}(0)]=12
\end{aligned}$$

\section*{PART - B}
1. If $\mathrm{x}=\cos \theta+\mathrm{i} \sin \theta$ find the value of $\mathrm{x}^{\mathrm{m}}+\frac{1}{\mathrm{x}^{\mathrm{m}}}$.

\section*{Solution:}

Given: $\mathrm{x}=\cos \theta+\mathrm{i} \sin \theta$
$$\begin{aligned}
& \Rightarrow \mathrm{x}^{\mathrm{m}}=(\cos \theta+\mathrm{i} \sin \theta)^{\mathrm{m}}=\cos \mathrm{m} \theta+\mathrm{i} \sin \mathrm{~m} \theta \\
& \text { also, } \frac{1}{\mathrm{x}^{\mathrm{m}}}=\left(\mathrm{x}^{\mathrm{m}}\right)^{-1} \\
& \quad=[\cos \mathrm{m} \theta+\mathrm{i} \sin \mathrm{~m} \theta]^{-1} \\
& \Rightarrow \frac{1}{\mathrm{x}^{\mathrm{m}}}=\cos \mathrm{m} \theta-\mathrm{i} \sin \mathrm{~m} \theta \\
& \therefore \mathrm{x}^{\mathrm{m}}+\frac{1}{\mathrm{x}^{\mathrm{m}}}=\cos \mathrm{m} \theta+\mathrm{i} \sin \mathrm{~m} \theta+\cos \mathrm{m} \theta-\mathrm{i} \sin \mathrm{~m} \theta=2 \cos \mathrm{~m} \theta
\end{aligned}$$
2. If $a=\cos \alpha+i \sin \alpha$ and $b=\cos \beta+i \sin \beta$ find $a b+\frac{1}{a b}$.

\section*{Solution:}

Given: $\mathrm{a}=\cos \alpha+\mathrm{i} \sin \alpha$
$$\& b=\cos \beta+i \sin \beta$$
$a b=(\cos \alpha+i \sin \alpha)(\cos \beta+i \sin \beta)$
$$\Rightarrow a b=\cos (\alpha+\beta)+i \sin (\alpha+\beta)$$
and $\frac{1}{\mathrm{ab}}=\cos (\alpha+\beta)-\mathrm{i} \sin (\alpha+\beta)$
$$\begin{aligned}
\therefore a b+\frac{1}{a b} & =\cos (\alpha+\beta)+i \sin (\alpha+\beta)+\cos (\alpha+\beta)-i \sin (\alpha+\beta) \\
& =2 \cos (\alpha+\beta)
\end{aligned}$$
3. Prove that $(\sin \theta+\mathrm{i} \cos \theta)^{\mathrm{n}}=\cos \mathrm{n}\left(\frac{\pi}{2}-\theta\right)+\mathrm{i} \sin \left(\frac{\pi}{2}-\theta\right)$.

\section*{Solution:}

We have $\quad \sin \theta=\cos \left(\frac{\pi}{2}-\theta\right)$
$$\begin{aligned}
& \& \cos \theta=\sin \left(\frac{\pi}{2}-\theta\right) \\
& \text { LHS: }(\sin \theta+i \cos \theta)^{n}=\left[\cos \left(\frac{\pi}{2}-\theta\right)+i \sin \left(\frac{\pi}{2}-\theta\right)\right]^{n} \\
&=\cos n\left(\frac{\pi}{2}-\theta\right)+i \sin n\left(\frac{\pi}{2}-\theta\right)
\end{aligned}$$
4. If $x=\cos 3 \alpha+i \sin 3 \alpha, y=\cos 3 \beta+i \sin 3 \beta$ find the value $\sqrt{\mathrm{ff}^{3}} \mathrm{xy}$.

\section*{Solution:}
$$\begin{aligned}
& \text { Given: } x=\cos 3 \alpha+i \sin 3 \alpha \\
& \quad \& y=\cos 3 \beta+i \sin 3 \beta \\
& x y=(\cos 3 \alpha+i \sin 3 \alpha)(\cos 3 \beta+i \sin 3 \beta) \\
& \quad=\cos (3 \alpha+3 \beta)+i \sin (3 \alpha+3 \beta) \\
& \Rightarrow x y=\cos 3(\alpha+\beta)+i \sin 3(\alpha+\beta) \\
& \sqrt[3]{\mathrm{xy}}=(x y)^{1 / 3} \\
& \quad=[\cos 3(\alpha+\beta)+i \sin 3(\alpha+\beta)]^{1 / 3} \\
& \quad=\cos \frac{1}{3} \cdot 3(\alpha+\beta)+i \sin \frac{1}{3} \cdot 3(\alpha+\beta) \\
& \quad=\cos (\alpha+\beta)+i \sin (\alpha+\beta)
\end{aligned}$$
5. If $\mathrm{a}=\cos \alpha+\mathrm{i} \sin \alpha, \mathrm{b}=\cos \beta+\mathrm{i} \sin \beta$ and $\mathrm{c}=\cos \gamma+\mathrm{i} \sin \gamma$ find the value of $\frac{\mathrm{ab}}{\mathrm{c}}$.

\section*{Solution:}

Given: $\mathrm{a}=\cos \alpha+\mathrm{i} \sin \alpha$
$$\begin{aligned}
& \quad \mathrm{b}=\cos \beta+i \sin \beta \\
& \& \mathrm{c}=\cos \gamma+\mathrm{i} \sin \gamma \\
& \therefore \frac{\mathrm{ab}}{\mathrm{c}}=\mathrm{ab}(\mathrm{c})^{-1} \\
& =(\cos \alpha+i \sin \alpha)(\cos \beta+i \sin \beta)(\cos \gamma+i \sin \gamma)^{-1} \\
& =(\cos \alpha+i \sin \alpha)(\cos \beta+i \sin \beta)(\cos \gamma-i \sin \gamma) \\
& \Rightarrow \frac{a b}{c}=\cos (\alpha+\beta-\gamma)+i \sin (\alpha+\beta-\gamma)
\end{aligned}$$

\section*{PART - C}
1. Simplify: $\frac{(\cos 2 \theta+\mathrm{i} \sin 2 \theta)^{3}(\cos 3 \theta-\mathrm{i} \sin 3 \theta)^{4}}{(\cos 3 \theta+\mathrm{i} \sin 3 \theta)^{2}(\cos 4 \theta+\mathrm{i} \sin 4 \theta)^{-3}}$

\section*{Solution:}
$\frac{(\cos 2 \theta+i \sin 2 \theta)^{3}(\cos 3 \theta-i \sin 3 \theta)^{4}}{(\cos 3 \theta+i \sin 3 \theta)^{2}(\cos 4 \theta+i \sin 4 \theta)^{-3}}$
$=\frac{(\cos \theta+\mathrm{i} \sin \theta)^{3 \times 2}(\cos \theta+\mathrm{i} \sin \theta)^{4 \times-3}}{(\cos \theta+\mathrm{i} \sin \theta)^{2 \times 3}(\cos \theta+\mathrm{i} \sin \theta)^{-3 \times 4}}$
$=\frac{(\cos \theta+\mathrm{i} \sin \theta)^{6}(\cos \theta+\mathrm{i} \sin \theta)^{-12}}{(\cos \theta+\mathrm{i} \sin \theta)^{6}(\cos \theta+\mathrm{i} \sin \theta)^{-12}}$
$=(\cos \theta+\mathrm{i} \sin \theta)^{6-12-6+12}$
$=(\cos \theta+\mathrm{i} \sin \theta)^{0}$
$=\cos 0+\mathrm{i} \sin 0$
$=1+\mathrm{i}(0)=1$
2. Simplify : $\frac{(\cos 2 \theta+\mathrm{i} \sin 2 \theta)^{3}(\cos 4 \theta-\mathrm{i} \sin 4 \theta)^{3}}{\cos 3 \theta+\mathrm{i} \sin 3 \theta}$ when $\theta=\frac{\pi}{9}$.

\section*{Solution:}
$$\begin{aligned}
& \frac{(\cos 2 \theta+\mathrm{i} \sin 2 \theta)^{3}(\cos 4 \theta-\mathrm{i} \sin 4 \theta)^{3}}{\cos 3 \theta+\mathrm{i} \sin 3 \theta} \\
& =\frac{(\cos \theta+\mathrm{i} \sin \theta)^{3 \times 2}(\cos \theta+\mathrm{i} \sin \theta)^{3 \times-4}}{(\cos \theta+\mathrm{i} \sin \theta)^{3}} \\
& =\frac{(\cos \theta+\mathrm{i} \sin \theta)^{6}(\cos \theta+\mathrm{i} \sin \theta)^{-12}}{(\cos \theta+\mathrm{i} \sin \theta)^{3}} \\
& =(\cos \theta+\mathrm{i} \sin \theta)^{6-12-3} \\
& =(\cos \theta+\mathrm{i} \sin \theta)^{-9} \\
& =\cos 9 \theta-\mathrm{i} \sin 9 \theta \quad \text { when } \theta=\frac{\pi}{9} \\
& =\cos 9\left(\frac{\pi}{9}\right)-\mathrm{i} \sin 9\left(\frac{\pi}{9}\right) \\
& =\cos \pi-\mathrm{i} \sin \pi \\
& =-1-\mathrm{i}(0)=-1
\end{aligned}$$
3. Prove that $\left(\frac{\cos \theta+\mathrm{i} \sin \theta}{\sin \theta+\mathrm{i} \cos \theta}\right)^{4}=\cos 8 \theta+\mathrm{i} \sin 8 \theta$.

\section*{Solution:}
$$\text { LHS : } \begin{aligned}
\left(\frac{\cos \theta+i \sin \theta}{\sin \theta+i \cos \theta}\right)^{4} & =\left[\frac{\cos \theta+i \sin \theta}{\sin \theta+i \cos \theta} \times \frac{i}{i}\right]^{4} \\
& =(i)^{4}\left[\frac{\cos \theta+i \sin \theta}{i \sin \theta+i^{2} \cos \theta}\right]^{4} \\
& =1\left[\frac{\cos \theta+i \sin \theta}{-\cos \theta+i \sin \theta}\right]^{4}
\end{aligned}$$
$$\begin{aligned}
& =\left[\frac{\cos \theta+i \sin \theta}{-(\cos \theta-i \sin \theta)}\right]^{4} \\
& =\left[\frac{\cos \theta+i \sin \theta}{(\cos \theta+i \sin \theta)^{-1}}\right]^{4} \\
& =\left[(\cos \theta+i \sin \theta)^{1+1}\right]^{4} \\
& =\left[(\cos \theta+i \sin \theta)^{2}\right]^{4} \\
& =(\cos \theta+i \sin \theta)^{8} \\
& =\cos 8 \theta+i \sin 8 \theta=\text { RHS }
\end{aligned}$$
4. Prove that $\left[\frac{1+\cos \theta+i \sin \theta}{1+\cos \theta-i \sin \theta}\right]^{n}=\cos n \theta+i \sin n \theta$.

\section*{Solution:}

Let $\mathrm{z}=\cos \theta+\mathrm{i} \sin \theta$
$$\begin{aligned}
& \Rightarrow \frac{1}{\mathrm{Z}}=\cos \theta-\mathrm{i} \sin \theta \\
& \text { LHS : }\left[\frac{1+\cos \theta+i \sin \theta}{1+\cos \theta-i \sin \theta}\right]^{\mathrm{n}} \\
& =\left[\frac{1+\mathrm{z}}{1+\frac{1}{\mathrm{z}}}\right]^{\mathrm{n}} \\
& =\left[\frac{1+z}{z+1}\right]^{\mathrm{z}} \\
& =\left[\frac{z(1+z)}{(1+z)}\right]^{\mathrm{n}} \\
& =\mathrm{z}^{\mathrm{n}} \\
& =(\cos \theta+\mathrm{i} \sin \theta)^{\mathrm{n}} \\
& =\cos n \theta+\mathrm{i} \sin \mathrm{n} \theta=\mathrm{RHS}
\end{aligned}$$
5. Show that $\left[\frac{1+\sin A+i \cos A}{1+\sin A-i \cos A}\right]^{n}=\cos n\left(\frac{\pi}{2}-A\right)+i \sin n\left(\frac{\pi}{2}-A\right)$.

\section*{Solution:}

Let $\mathrm{z}=\sin \mathrm{A}+\mathrm{i} \cos \mathrm{A}$
$\Rightarrow \mathrm{z}=\cos \left(\frac{\pi}{2}-\mathrm{A}\right)+\mathrm{i} \sin \left(\frac{\pi}{2}-\mathrm{A}\right)$
$\therefore \frac{1}{\mathrm{Z}}=\cos \left(\frac{\pi}{2}-\mathrm{A}\right)-\mathrm{i} \sin \left(\frac{\pi}{2}-\mathrm{A}\right)=\sin \mathrm{A}-\mathrm{i} \cos \mathrm{A}$
$$\begin{aligned}
& \text { LHS }\left[\frac{1+\sin A+i \cos A}{1+\sin A-i \cos A}\right]^{\mathrm{n}} \\
& \left.\begin{array}{rl}
=\left[\frac{1+\mathrm{z}}{1+\frac{1}{\mathrm{z}}}\right]^{\mathrm{n}} \\
& =\left[\frac{1+\mathrm{z}}{\mathrm{z}+1}\right]^{\mathrm{n}}
\end{array}\right] \\
& \quad=\left[\frac{\mathrm{z}(1+\mathrm{z})}{(1+\mathrm{z})}\right]^{\mathrm{n}} \\
& \\
& =(\mathrm{z})^{\mathrm{n}} \\
& = \\
& =\left[\cos \left(\frac{\pi}{2}-\mathrm{A}\right)+\mathrm{i} \sin \left(\frac{\pi}{2}-\mathrm{A}\right)\right]^{\mathrm{n}} \\
& =\cos n\left(\frac{\pi}{2}-\mathrm{A}\right)+\mathrm{i} \sin n\left(\frac{\pi}{2}-\mathrm{A}\right)
\end{aligned}$$
6. If $\mathrm{a}=\cos \theta+\mathrm{i} \sin \theta, \mathrm{b}=\cos \phi+i \sin \phi$ prove that
(i) $\cos (\theta+\phi)=\frac{1}{2}\left[a b+\frac{1}{a b}\right]$
(ii) $\sin (\theta-\phi)=\frac{1}{2 \mathrm{i}}\left[\frac{\mathrm{a}}{\mathrm{b}}-\frac{\mathrm{b}}{\mathrm{a}}\right]$

Solution:
Given $\mathrm{a}=\cos \theta+\mathrm{i} \sin \theta$
$$\& \quad b=\cos \phi+i \sin \phi$$

Now, $\mathrm{ab}=(\cos \theta+\mathrm{i} \sin \theta)(\cos \phi+\mathrm{i} \sin \phi)$
$\Rightarrow \mathrm{ab}=\cos (\theta+\phi)+\mathrm{i} \sin (\theta+\phi)$
also, $\frac{1}{\mathrm{ab}}=(\mathrm{ab})^{-1}=[\cos (\theta+\phi)+\mathrm{i} \sin (\theta+\phi)]$
$$\Rightarrow \frac{1}{\mathrm{ab}}=\cos (\theta+\phi)-\mathrm{i} \sin (\theta+\phi)$$
$\therefore(1)+(2) \Rightarrow$
$\mathrm{ab}+\frac{1}{\mathrm{ab}}=\cos (\theta+\phi)+\mathrm{i} \sin (\theta+\phi)+\cos (\theta+\phi)-\mathrm{i} \sin (\theta+\phi)$
$\Rightarrow \mathrm{ab}+\frac{1}{\mathrm{ab}}=2 \cos (\theta+\phi)$
$\Rightarrow \cos (\theta+\phi)=\frac{1}{2}\left[a b+\frac{1}{a b}\right]$
(ii) $\frac{\mathrm{a}}{\mathrm{b}}=\mathrm{a}(\mathrm{b})^{-1}$
$=(\cos \theta+i \sin \theta)(\cos \phi+i \sin \phi)^{-1}$
$=(\cos \theta+\mathrm{i} \sin \theta)(\cos \phi-\mathrm{i} \sin \phi)$
$\Rightarrow \frac{\mathrm{a}}{\mathrm{b}}=\cos (\theta-\phi)+\mathrm{i} \sin (\theta-\phi)$.
also, $\frac{\mathrm{b}}{\mathrm{a}}=\left(\frac{\mathrm{a}}{\mathrm{b}}\right)^{-1}=[\cos (\theta-\phi)+\mathrm{i} \sin (\theta-\phi)]^{-1}$
$\Rightarrow \frac{\mathrm{b}}{\mathrm{a}}=\cos (\theta-\phi)-\mathrm{i} \sin (\theta-\phi)$
(3) $-(4) \Rightarrow$
$\frac{\mathrm{a}}{\mathrm{b}}-\frac{\mathrm{b}}{\mathrm{a}}=\cos (\theta-\phi)+\mathrm{i} \sin (\theta-\phi)-\cos (\theta-\phi)+\mathrm{i} \sin (\theta-\phi)$
$\Rightarrow \frac{\mathrm{a}}{\mathrm{b}}-\frac{\mathrm{b}}{\mathrm{a}}=2 \mathrm{i} \sin (\theta-\phi)$
$\Rightarrow \sin (\theta-\phi)=\frac{1}{2 \mathrm{i}}\left[\frac{\mathrm{a}}{\mathrm{b}}-\frac{\mathrm{b}}{\mathrm{a}}\right]$
7. If $a=\cos x+i \sin x, b=\cos y+i \sin y$ prove that
(i) $\sqrt{\mathrm{ab}}+\frac{1}{\sqrt{\mathrm{ab}}}=2 \cos \left(\frac{x+y}{2}\right)$
(ii) $\sqrt{\frac{a}{b}}+\sqrt{\frac{b}{a}}=2 \cos \left(\frac{x-y}{2}\right)$

\section*{Solution:}

Given: $\mathrm{a}=\cos \mathrm{x}+\mathrm{i} \sin \mathrm{x}$
$$\& b=\cos y+i \sin y$$
(i) Now, $\mathrm{ab}=(\cos \mathrm{x}+\mathrm{i} \sin \mathrm{x})(\cos \mathrm{y}+\mathrm{i} \sin \mathrm{y})$
$\Rightarrow a b=\cos (x+y)+i \sin (x+y)$
$\therefore \sqrt{a b}=(a b)^{1 / 2}=[\cos (x+y)+i \sin (x+y)]^{1 / 2}$
$\Rightarrow \sqrt{\mathrm{ab}}=\cos \left(\frac{\mathrm{x}+\mathrm{y}}{2}\right)+\mathrm{i} \sin \left(\frac{\mathrm{x}+\mathrm{y}}{2}\right)$
also, $\frac{1}{\sqrt{a b}}=(\sqrt{a b})^{-1}=\left[\cos \left(\frac{x+y}{2}\right)+i \sin \left(\frac{x+y}{2}\right)\right]^{-1}$
$\Rightarrow \frac{1}{\sqrt{a b}}=\cos \left(\frac{x+y}{2}\right)-i \sin \left(\frac{x+y}{2}\right)$
$\therefore(1)+(2) \Rightarrow$
$\sqrt{a b}+\frac{1}{\sqrt{a b}}=\cos \left(\frac{x+y}{2}\right)+i \sin \left(\frac{x+y}{2}\right)+\cos \left(\frac{x+y}{2}\right)-i \sin \left(\frac{x+y}{2}\right)$
$\Rightarrow \sqrt{\mathrm{ab}}+\frac{1}{\sqrt{\mathrm{ab}}}=2 \cos \left(\frac{\mathrm{x}+\mathrm{y}}{2}\right)$
$$\begin{aligned}
\text { (ii) } \begin{aligned}
\frac{\mathrm{a}}{\mathrm{~b}}=\mathrm{a}(\mathrm{~b})^{-1} & =(\cos \mathrm{x}+\mathrm{i} \sin \mathrm{x})(\cos \mathrm{y}+\mathrm{i} \sin \mathrm{y})^{-1} \\
& =(\cos \mathrm{x}+\mathrm{i} \sin \mathrm{x})(\cos \mathrm{y}-\mathrm{i} \sin \mathrm{y}) \\
\Rightarrow \quad \frac{\mathrm{a}}{\mathrm{~b}} & =\cos (\mathrm{x}-\mathrm{y})+\mathrm{i} \sin (\mathrm{x}-\mathrm{y})
\end{aligned}
\end{aligned}$$
$$\begin{aligned}
& \therefore \sqrt{\frac{a}{b}}=\left(\frac{a}{b}\right)^{1 / 2}=[\cos (x-y)+i \sin (x-y)]^{1 / 2} \\
& \Rightarrow \quad \sqrt{\frac{a}{b}}=\cos \left(\frac{x-y}{2}\right)+i \sin \left(\frac{x-y}{2}\right) \ldots \ldots
\end{aligned}$$
also, $\quad \sqrt{\frac{b}{a}}=\left(\sqrt{\frac{a}{b}}\right)^{-1}=\left[\cos \left(\frac{x-y}{2}\right)+i \sin \left(\frac{x-y}{2}\right)\right]^{-1}$
$$\Rightarrow \quad \sqrt{\frac{b}{a}}=\cos \left(\frac{x-y}{2}\right)-i \sin \left(\frac{x-y}{2}\right)$$
$\therefore(3)+(4) \Rightarrow$
$$\begin{aligned}
& \sqrt{\frac{a}{b}}+\sqrt{\frac{b}{a}}=\cos \left(\frac{x-y}{2}\right)+i \sin \left(\frac{x-y}{2}\right)+\cos \left(\frac{x-y}{2}\right)-i \sin \left(\frac{x-y}{2}\right) \\
& \Rightarrow \sqrt{\frac{a}{b}}+\sqrt{\frac{b}{a}}=2 \cos \left(\frac{x-y}{2}\right)
\end{aligned}$$
8. If $\mathrm{a}=\cos \alpha+\mathrm{i} \sin \alpha, \mathrm{b}=\cos \beta+\mathrm{i} \sin \beta$ and $\mathrm{c}=\cos \gamma+\mathrm{i} \sin \gamma$ find the value of $\frac{\mathrm{ab}}{\mathrm{c}}-\frac{\mathrm{c}}{\mathrm{ab}}$.

\section*{Solution:}

Given:
$$\begin{aligned}
& \mathrm{a}=\cos \alpha+i \sin \alpha \\
& \mathrm{~b}=\cos \beta+i \sin \beta
\end{aligned}$$
\& $c=\cos \gamma+i \sin \gamma$
Now,
$$\begin{aligned}
\frac{\mathrm{ab}}{\mathrm{c}}= & \mathrm{ab}(\mathrm{c})^{-1} \\
& =(\cos \alpha+\mathrm{i} \sin \alpha)(\cos \beta+\mathrm{i} \sin \beta)(\cos \gamma+ \\
& =(\cos \alpha+\mathrm{i} \sin \alpha)(\cos \beta+\mathrm{i} \sin \beta)(\cos \gamma- \\
\Rightarrow \quad \frac{\mathrm{ab}}{\mathrm{c}} & =\cos (\alpha+\beta-\gamma)+i \sin (\alpha+\beta-\gamma) \ldots . .(1) \\
\text { also, } \frac{\mathrm{c}}{\mathrm{ab}} & =\left[\frac{\mathrm{ab}}{\mathrm{c}}\right]^{-1} \\
& =[\cos (\alpha+\beta-\gamma)+\mathrm{i} \sin (\alpha+\beta-\gamma)]^{-1} \\
\Rightarrow \frac{c}{\mathrm{ab}} & =\cos (\alpha+\beta-\gamma)-i \sin (\alpha+\beta-\gamma)
\end{aligned}$$
$\therefore(1)-(2) \Rightarrow$
$$\begin{aligned}
& \frac{\mathrm{ab}}{\mathrm{c}}-\frac{\mathrm{c}}{\mathrm{ab}}=\cos (\alpha+\beta-\gamma)+\mathrm{i} \sin (\alpha+\beta-\gamma)-\cos (\alpha+\beta-\gamma)+i \sin (\alpha+\beta-\gamma) \\
\Rightarrow & \frac{\mathrm{ab}}{\mathrm{c}}-\frac{\mathrm{c}}{\mathrm{ab}}=2 \operatorname{isin}(\alpha+\beta-\gamma)
\end{aligned}$$
9. If $\mathrm{x}+\frac{1}{\mathrm{x}}=2 \cos \theta$ prove that (i) $\mathrm{x}^{\mathrm{n}}+\frac{1}{\mathrm{x}^{\mathrm{n}}}=2 \cos \mathrm{n} \theta \quad$ (ii) $\mathrm{x}^{\mathrm{n}}-\frac{1}{\mathrm{x}^{\mathrm{n}}}=2 \mathrm{i} \sin \mathrm{n} \theta$.

\section*{Solution:}

Given: $\mathrm{x}+\frac{1}{\mathrm{x}}=2 \cos \theta$
$$\begin{aligned}
& \frac{x^{2}+1}{x}=2 \cos \theta \\
& x^{2}+1=2 x \cos \theta \\
& x^{2}-2 x \cos \theta+1=0
\end{aligned}$$

Here $\mathrm{a}=1, \mathrm{~b}=-2 \cos \theta \& \mathrm{c}=1$
$$\begin{aligned}
\therefore & x=\frac{-\mathrm{b} \pm \sqrt{\mathrm{b}^{2}-4 \mathrm{ac}}}{2 \mathrm{a}} \\
& =\frac{2 \cos \theta \pm \sqrt{(-2 \cos \theta)^{2}-4 \times 1 \times 1}}{2 \times 1} \\
& =\frac{2 \cos \theta \pm \sqrt{4 \cos ^{2} \theta-4}}{2} \\
& =\frac{2 \cos \theta \pm \sqrt{4\left(\cos ^{2} \theta-1\right)}}{2} \\
& =\frac{2 \cos \theta \pm \sqrt{4\left(-\sin ^{2} \theta\right)}}{2} \\
& =\frac{2 \cos \theta \pm \mathrm{i} 2 \sin \theta}{2} \\
& =\frac{2[\cos \theta \pm \mathrm{i} \sin \theta]}{2} \\
\Rightarrow \mathrm{x} & =\cos \theta \pm \mathrm{i} \sin \theta
\end{aligned}$$

Consider $\mathrm{x}=\cos \theta+\mathrm{i} \sin \theta$
$\therefore \mathrm{x}^{\mathrm{n}}=(\cos \theta+\mathrm{i} \sin \theta)^{\mathrm{n}}=\cos \mathrm{n} \theta+\mathrm{i} \sin \mathrm{n} \theta$
$\& \frac{1}{\mathrm{x}^{\mathrm{n}}}=\cos \mathrm{n} \theta-\mathrm{i} \sin \mathrm{n} \theta$
(i) $\mathrm{x}^{\mathrm{n}}+\frac{1}{\mathrm{x}^{\mathrm{n}}}=\cos \mathrm{n} \theta+\mathrm{i} \sin \mathrm{n} \theta+\cos \mathrm{n} \theta-\mathrm{i} \sin \mathrm{n} \theta$
$\Rightarrow \mathrm{x}^{\mathrm{n}}+\frac{1}{\mathrm{x}^{\mathrm{n}}}=2 \cos \mathrm{n} \theta$
(ii) $\mathrm{x}^{\mathrm{n}}-\frac{1}{\mathrm{x}^{\mathrm{n}}}=\cos \mathrm{n} \theta+\mathrm{i} \sin \mathrm{n} \theta-\cos \mathrm{n} \theta+\mathrm{i} \sin \mathrm{n} \theta$
$\Rightarrow \mathrm{x}^{\mathrm{n}}-\frac{1}{\mathrm{x}^{\mathrm{n}}}=2 \mathrm{i} \sin \mathrm{n} \theta$
10. Show that $(1+i)^{n}+(1-i)^{n}=2^{\frac{n+2}{2}} \cos \frac{n \pi}{4}$.

\section*{Solution:}

Let $1+\mathrm{i}=\mathrm{r}(\cos \theta+\mathrm{i} \sin \theta)=\mathrm{r} \cos \theta+\mathrm{i} \sin \theta$
Equating real \& imaginary parts on both sides
$$r \cos \theta=1 \quad \& \quad r \sin \theta=1$$

Now, $(\mathrm{rcos} \theta)^{2}+(\mathrm{r} \sin \theta)^{2}=(1)^{2}+(1)^{2}$
$$\begin{array}{rrr}
\Rightarrow & \mathrm{r}^{2} \cos ^{2} \theta+\mathrm{r}^{2} \sin ^{2} \theta=1+1 \\
\Rightarrow & \mathrm{r}^{2}\left(\cos ^{2} \theta+\sin ^{2} \theta\right)=2 \\
\Rightarrow & \mathrm{r}^{2}(1) & =2 \\
\Rightarrow & \mathrm{r}^{2}=2 \quad \Rightarrow \quad r=\sqrt{2}
\end{array}$$

Also, $\frac{\mathrm{r} \sin \theta}{\mathrm{r} \cos \theta}=\frac{1}{1}$
$\Rightarrow \tan \theta=1$
$\Rightarrow \theta=\tan ^{-1}(1)$
$\Rightarrow \theta=\frac{\pi}{4}$
$\therefore 1+\mathrm{i}=\sqrt{2}\left[\cos \frac{\pi}{4}+\mathrm{i} \sin \frac{\pi}{4}\right]$
Similarly we can prove that
$1-\mathrm{i}=\sqrt{2}\left[\cos \frac{\pi}{4}-\mathrm{i} \sin \frac{\pi}{4}\right]$.
LHS: $(1+i)^{n}+(1-i)^{n}$
$=\left[\sqrt{2}\left(\cos \frac{\pi}{4}+\mathrm{i} \sin \frac{\pi}{4}\right)\right]^{\mathrm{n}}+\left[\sqrt{2}\left(\cos \frac{\pi}{4}-\mathrm{i} \sin \frac{\pi}{4}\right)\right]^{\mathrm{n}}$
$=(\sqrt{2})^{\mathrm{n}}\left[\cos \frac{\pi}{4}+\mathrm{i} \sin \frac{\pi}{4}\right]^{\mathrm{n}}+(\sqrt{2})^{\mathrm{n}}\left[\cos \frac{\pi}{4}-\mathrm{i} \sin \frac{\pi}{4}\right]^{\mathrm{n}}$
$=(\sqrt{2})^{\mathrm{n}}\left[\cos \frac{\mathrm{n} \pi}{4}+\mathrm{i} \sin \frac{\mathrm{n} \pi}{4}+\cos \frac{\mathrm{n} \pi}{4}-\mathrm{i} \sin \frac{\mathrm{n} \pi}{4}\right]$
$=2^{\frac{n}{2}} 2 \cos \frac{n \pi}{4}$
$=2^{\frac{n}{2}+1} \cos \frac{n \pi}{4}$
$=2^{\frac{n+2}{2}} \cos \frac{n \pi}{4}=$ RHS

\subsection*{2.3 ROOTS OF COMPLEX NUMBERS}

\section*{Definition:}

A number $\omega$ is called the $\mathrm{n}^{\text {th }}$ root of a complex number z , if $\omega^{\mathrm{n}}=\mathrm{z}$ and we write $\omega=\mathrm{z}^{\frac{1}{n}}$.
Working rule to find the $n^{\text {th }}$ roots of a complex numbers:

Step (I) : Write the given complex number in Polar form.
Step (II) : Add " $2 \mathrm{k} \pi$ " to the argument.
Step (III) : Apply Demoivre's theorem
Step (IV) : Put k=0, $1, \ldots \ldots$. upto ( $\mathrm{n}-1$ ).

\section*{Illustration:}

Let $z=r(\cos \theta+i \sin \theta)$
$$\begin{aligned}
\Rightarrow z & =r[\cos (2 k \pi+\theta)+i \sin (2 \mathrm{k} \pi+\theta)] \quad \text { where } k \in I \\
\therefore z^{\frac{1}{n}} & =\{r[\cos (2 k \pi+\theta)+i \sin (2 \mathrm{k} \pi+\theta)]\}^{\frac{1}{n}} \\
& =r^{\frac{1}{n}}[\cos (2 \mathrm{k} \pi+\theta)+i \sin (2 \mathrm{k} \pi+\theta)]^{\frac{1}{n}} \\
& =r^{\frac{1}{n}}\left[\cos \left(\frac{2 \mathrm{k} \pi+\theta}{\mathrm{n}}\right)+\mathrm{i} \sin \left(\frac{2 \mathrm{k} \pi+\theta}{\mathrm{n}}\right)\right] \text { where } \mathrm{K}=0,1,2, \ldots \ldots . ., \mathrm{n}-1 .
\end{aligned}$$

Only these values of $k$ will give ' $n$ ' different values of $z^{\frac{1}{n}}$ provided $z \neq 0$.

\section*{To find the $\mathrm{n}^{\text {th }}$ roots of unity}
$1=\cos 0+\mathrm{i} \sin 0=\cos 2 \mathrm{k} \pi+\mathrm{i} \sin 2 \mathrm{k} \pi$
$\therefore \mathrm{n}^{\text {th }}$ roots of unity $=1^{1 / \mathrm{n}}=(\cos 2 \mathrm{k} \pi+\mathrm{i} \sin 2 \mathrm{k} \pi)^{1 / \mathrm{n}}$
$$=\cos \left(\frac{2 \mathrm{k} \pi}{\mathrm{n}}\right)+\mathrm{i} \sin \left(\frac{2 \mathrm{k} \pi}{\mathrm{n}}\right) \text { where } \mathrm{k}=0,1,2, \ldots \ldots \ldots \ldots \ldots, \mathrm{n}-1$$

The roots are,
for $k=0 ; \quad R_{1}=\cos 0+i \sin 0=1+i 0=1=e^{i 0}$
$$\begin{aligned}
& \mathrm{k}=1 ; \quad \mathrm{R}_{2}=\cos \frac{2 \pi}{\mathrm{n}}+\mathrm{i} \sin \frac{2 \pi}{\mathrm{n}}=\mathrm{e}^{\mathrm{i} \frac{2 \pi}{\mathrm{n}}}=\omega \text { (say) } \\
& \mathrm{k}=2 ; \quad \mathrm{R}_{3}=\cos \frac{4 \pi}{\mathrm{n}}+\mathrm{i} \sin \frac{4 \pi}{\mathrm{n}}=\mathrm{e}^{\mathrm{i} \frac{4 \pi}{\mathrm{n}}}=\left[\mathrm{e}^{\left.\mathrm{i}^{\frac{2 \pi}{n}}\right]^{2}}=\omega^{2}\right. \\
& \mathrm{k}=\mathrm{n}-1 ; \mathrm{R}_{\mathrm{n}}=\cos \frac{2(\mathrm{n}-1) \pi}{\mathrm{n}}+\mathrm{i} \sin \frac{2(\mathrm{n}-1) \pi}{\mathrm{n}}=\mathrm{e}^{\mathrm{i} \frac{2(\mathrm{n}-1) \pi}{\mathrm{n}}}=\omega^{\mathrm{n}-1}
\end{aligned}$$
$\therefore$ The $\mathrm{n}^{\text {th }}$ roots of unity are
$e^{i 0}, e^{i \frac{2 \pi}{n}}, e^{i \frac{4 \pi}{n}}, \ldots \ldots \ldots, e^{i \frac{2(n-1) \pi}{n}}$
i.e., $1, \omega, \omega^{2}, \ldots \ldots \ldots . ., \omega^{\mathrm{n}-1}$.

\section*{Result:}

If $\omega$ is $n^{\text {th }}$ roots of unity then
(i) $\omega^{\mathrm{n}}=1$
(ii) Sum of the roots is zero.
i.e $1+\omega+\omega^{2}+$ $+\omega^{\mathrm{n}-1}=0$
(iii) The roots are in G.P with common ratio $\omega$.
(iv) The arguments are in A.P with common difference $\frac{2 \pi}{n}$.
(v) The product of the roots is $(-1)^{n+1}$.

\section*{To find cube roots of unity}

Let $\mathrm{x}=(1)^{1 / 3}$
$$\begin{aligned}
& =(\cos 0+\mathrm{i} \sin 0)^{1 / 3} \\
& =(\cos 2 \mathrm{k} \pi+\mathrm{i} \sin 2 \mathrm{k} \pi)^{1 / 3} \\
& =\cos \left(\frac{2 \mathrm{k} \pi}{3}\right)+\mathrm{i} \sin \left(\frac{2 \mathrm{k} \pi}{3}\right) \text { where } \mathrm{k}=0,1,2
\end{aligned}$$
$\therefore$ The roots are
$$\begin{aligned}
\text { for } \mathrm{k}=0 ; & \mathrm{R}_{1}=\cos 0+\mathrm{i} \sin 0=1+\mathrm{i} 0=1 \\
\mathrm{k}=1 ; & \mathrm{R}_{2}=\cos \frac{2 \pi}{3}+\mathrm{i} \sin \frac{2 \pi}{3}=-\frac{1}{2}+\mathrm{i} \frac{\sqrt{3}}{2} \\
\mathrm{k}=2 ; & \mathrm{R}_{3}=\cos \frac{4 \pi}{3}+\mathrm{i} \sin \frac{4 \pi}{3}=-\frac{1}{2}-\mathrm{i} \frac{\sqrt{3}}{2}
\end{aligned}$$

The cube roots of unity are $1,-\frac{1}{2}+i \frac{\sqrt{3}}{2},-\frac{1}{2}-i \frac{\sqrt{3}}{2}$.

\section*{Result:}

If we denote the second root $\mathrm{R}_{2}=\cos \frac{2 \pi}{3}+\mathrm{i} \sin \frac{2 \pi}{3}$ by $\omega$ then the other root, $\mathrm{R}_{3}=\cos \frac{4 \pi}{3}+\mathrm{i} \sin \frac{4 \pi}{3}$ becomes $\omega^{2}$
Thus, $\mathrm{R}_{2}=\cos \frac{2 \pi}{3}+\mathrm{i} \sin \frac{2 \pi}{3}=-\frac{1}{2}+\mathrm{i} \frac{\sqrt{3}}{2}=\omega$
$$\mathrm{R}_{3}=\cos \frac{4 \pi}{3}+\mathrm{i} \sin \frac{4 \pi}{3}=-\frac{1}{2}-\mathrm{i} \frac{\sqrt{3}}{2}=\omega^{2}$$
$\therefore$ The cube roots of unit are $1, \omega, \omega^{2}$

\section*{Note:}

If $\omega$ is cube roots of unity then (i) $\omega^{3}=1$, (ii) $1+\omega+\omega^{2}=0$

\section*{Fourth roots of unity}

Let $\mathrm{x}=(1)^{1 / 4}$
$$\begin{aligned}
& =(\cos 0+\mathrm{i} \sin 0)^{1 / 4} \\
& =(\cos 2 \mathrm{k} \pi+\mathrm{i} \sin 2 \mathrm{k} \pi)^{1 / 4} \\
& =\cos \left(\frac{2 \mathrm{k} \pi}{4}\right)+\mathrm{i} \sin \left(\frac{2 \mathrm{k} \pi}{4}\right) \text { where } \mathrm{k}=0,1,2,3
\end{aligned}$$
$\therefore$ The roots are,
for $\mathrm{k}=0 ; \quad \mathrm{R}_{1}=\cos 0+\mathrm{i} \sin 0=1+\mathrm{i} 0=1$
$$\begin{aligned}
& \mathrm{k}=1 ; \quad \mathrm{R}_{2}=\cos \frac{\pi}{2}+\mathrm{i} \sin \frac{\pi}{2}=0+\mathrm{i}(1)=\mathrm{i}=\omega(\text { say }) \\
& \mathrm{k}=2 ; \\
& \mathrm{R} \mathrm{R}_{3}=\cos \pi+\mathrm{i} \sin \pi=-1+\mathrm{i}(0)=-1=\omega^{2} \\
& \mathrm{k}=3 ; \mathrm{R}_{4}=\cos \frac{3 \pi}{2}+\mathrm{i} \sin \frac{3 \pi}{2}=0+\mathrm{i}(-1)=-\mathrm{i}=\omega
\end{aligned}$$

The fourth roots of unity are $1, i,-1,-\mathrm{i}$ (i.e.) $1, \omega, \omega^{2}, \omega^{3}$.

\section*{Note:}
(i) The sum of the fourth roots of unity is zero. i.e $1+\omega+\omega^{2}+\omega^{3}=0$ and $\omega^{4}=1$.
(ii) The value of $\omega$ used in cube roots of untiy and in fourth roots of unity are different.

\section*{Sixth roots of unity}

Let $\mathrm{x}=(1)^{1 / 6}$
$$\begin{aligned}
& =(\cos 0+\mathrm{i} \sin 0)^{1 / 6} \\
& =(\cos 2 \mathrm{k} \pi+\mathrm{i} \sin 2 \mathrm{k} \pi)^{1 / 6} \\
& =\cos \left(\frac{2 \mathrm{k} \pi}{6}\right)+\mathrm{i} \sin \left(\frac{2 \mathrm{k} \pi}{6}\right) \text { where } \mathrm{k}=0,1,2,3,4,5
\end{aligned}$$
$\therefore$ The six roots are
$$\begin{array}{rlrl}
\text { for } \mathrm{k}=0 ; & \quad \mathrm{R}_{1}=\cos 0+\mathrm{i} \sin 0=\mathrm{e}^{\mathrm{i} 0}=1 \\
\mathrm{k} & =1 ; & \mathrm{R}_{2}=\cos \frac{2 \pi}{6}+\mathrm{i} \sin \frac{2 \pi}{6}=\mathrm{e}^{\mathrm{i} \frac{2 \pi}{6}}=\omega \\
\mathrm{k} & =2 ; & \mathrm{R}_{3}=\cos \frac{4 \pi}{6}+\mathrm{i} \sin \frac{4 \pi}{6}=\mathrm{e}^{\mathrm{i} \frac{4 \pi}{6}}=\omega^{2} \\
\mathrm{k} & =3 ; & \mathrm{R}_{4}=\cos \frac{6 \pi}{6}+\mathrm{i} \sin \frac{6 \pi}{6}=\mathrm{e}^{\mathrm{i} \frac{6 \pi}{6}}=\omega^{3} \\
\mathrm{k}=4 ; & \mathrm{R}_{5}=\cos \frac{8 \pi}{6}+\mathrm{i} \sin \frac{8 \pi}{6}=\mathrm{e}^{\mathrm{i} \frac{8 \pi}{6}}=\omega^{4} \\
\mathrm{k}=5 ; & \mathrm{R}_{6}=\cos \frac{10 \pi}{6}+\mathrm{i} \sin \frac{10 \pi}{6}=e^{\mathrm{i} \frac{10 \pi}{6}}=\omega^{5}
\end{array}$$
$\therefore$ The sixth roots of unity are $\mathrm{e}^{\mathrm{i} 0}, \mathrm{e}^{\mathrm{i} \frac{2 \pi}{6}}, \mathrm{e}^{\mathrm{i} \frac{4 \pi}{6}}, \mathrm{e}^{\mathrm{i} \frac{6 \pi}{6}}, \mathrm{e}^{\mathrm{i} \frac{8 \pi}{6}}, \mathrm{e}^{\mathrm{i} \frac{10 \pi}{6}}$
i.e, $1, \omega, \omega^{2}, \omega^{3}, \omega^{4}, \omega^{5}$.

\section*{Note:}

The sum of the sixth roots of unity is zero. i.e $1+\omega+\omega^{2}+\omega^{3}+\omega^{4}+\omega^{5}=0$ and $\omega^{6}=1$
Note: $1=\cos 0+i \sin 0$
$$\begin{aligned}
&-1=\cos \pi+i \sin \pi \\
& i=\cos \frac{\pi}{2}+i \sin \frac{\pi}{2} \\
&-i=\cos \frac{3 \pi}{2}+i \sin \frac{3 \pi}{2}
\end{aligned}$$

\section*{WORKED EXAMPLES}

\section*{PART - A}
1. If $\omega$ is a cube roots of unity, find the value of $\omega^{4}+\omega^{5}+\omega^{6}$.

\section*{Solution:}

If $\omega$ is cube roots of unity then $\omega^{3}=1$.
$$\begin{aligned}
\therefore \omega^{4}+\omega^{5}+\omega^{6} & =\omega^{3} \cdot \omega+\omega^{3} \cdot \omega^{2}+\omega^{3} \cdot \omega^{3} \\
& =(1) \omega+(1) \omega^{2}+(1)(1) \\
& =\omega+\omega^{2}+1 \\
& =1+\omega+\omega^{2}=0
\end{aligned}$$
2. Simplify: $(1+\omega)\left(1+\omega^{2}\right)$ where $\omega$ is cube roots of unity.

\section*{Solution:}
$$\begin{aligned}
(1+\omega)\left(1+\omega^{2}\right) & =1+\omega^{2}+\omega+\omega^{3} \\
& =\left[1+\omega+\omega^{2}\right]+\omega^{3} \\
& =0+1 \\
& =1
\end{aligned}$$
3. Solve: $\mathrm{x}^{2}-1=0$

\section*{Solution:}

Given: $\mathrm{x}^{2}-1=0$
$$\begin{aligned}
\mathrm{x}^{2} & =1 \\
\mathrm{x} & =(1)^{1 / 2}=[\cos 0+\mathrm{i} \sin 0]^{1 / 2} \\
& =[\cos 2 \mathrm{k} \pi+\mathrm{i} \sin 2 \mathrm{k} \pi]^{1 / 2} \\
& =\cos \left(\frac{2 \mathrm{k} \pi}{2}\right)+\mathrm{i} \sin \left(\frac{2 \mathrm{k} \pi}{2}\right) \text { where } \mathrm{k}=0,1
\end{aligned}$$
4. Find the value of $\left(i^{1 / 3}\right.$.

\section*{Solution:}

Let $\mathrm{x}=(\mathrm{i})^{1 / 3}$
$$\begin{aligned}
& =\left[\cos \frac{\pi}{2}+\mathrm{i} \sin \frac{\pi}{2}\right]^{1 / 3} \\
& =\left[\cos \left(2 \mathrm{k} \pi+\frac{\pi}{2}\right)+\mathrm{i} \sin \left(2 \mathrm{k} \pi+\frac{\pi}{2}\right)\right]^{1 / 3} \\
& =\cos \frac{1}{3}\left(2 \mathrm{k} \pi+\frac{\pi}{2}\right)+\mathrm{i} \sin \frac{1}{3}\left(2 \mathrm{k} \pi+\frac{\pi}{2}\right) \quad \text { where } \mathrm{k}=0,1,2 .
\end{aligned}$$
5. Find the value of $(-1)^{1 / 3}$.

\section*{Solution:}

Let $x=(-1)^{1 / 3}$
$$\begin{aligned}
& =(\cos \pi+\mathrm{i} \sin \pi)^{1 / 3} \\
& =[\cos (2 \mathrm{k} \pi+\pi)+\mathrm{i} \sin (2 \mathrm{k} \pi+\pi)]^{1 / 3} \\
& =\cos \frac{1}{3}(2 \mathrm{k} \pi+\pi)+\operatorname{isin} \frac{1}{3}(2 \mathrm{k} \pi+\pi) \text { where } \mathrm{k}=0,1,2
\end{aligned}$$
6. Find the value of $\left(\frac{-1+i \sqrt{3}}{2}\right)^{3}$.

\section*{Solution:}

We have $\frac{-1+i \sqrt{3}}{2}=\frac{-1}{2}+i \frac{\sqrt{3}}{2}=\cos 120^{\circ}+i \sin 120^{\circ}$
$$\begin{aligned}
\left(\frac{-1+\mathrm{i} \sqrt{3}}{2}\right)^{3} & =\left(\cos 120^{\circ}+\mathrm{i} \sin 120^{\circ}\right)^{3} \\
& =\cos 360^{\circ}+\mathrm{i} \sin 360^{\circ} \\
& =1+\mathrm{i}(0) \\
& =1
\end{aligned}$$

\section*{PART - B}
1. Find the cube roots of unity.

\section*{Solution:}

Let ' $x$ ' be the cube roots of unity.
i.e. $x^{3}=1$
$$\begin{aligned}
\mathrm{x} & =(1)^{1 / 3} \\
& =(\cos 0+i \sin 0)^{1 / 3} \\
& =(\cos 2 \mathrm{k} \pi+i \sin 2 \mathrm{k} \pi)^{1 / 3} \\
& =\cos \frac{2 \mathrm{k} \pi}{3}+i \sin \frac{2 \mathrm{k} \pi}{3} \text { where } \mathrm{k}=0,1,2
\end{aligned}$$

For $k=0 ; x=\cos 0+i \sin 0=1+i 0=1$
$$\begin{aligned}
& \mathrm{k}=1 ; \mathrm{x}=\cos \frac{2 \pi}{3}+\mathrm{i} \sin \frac{2 \pi}{3} \\
& \mathrm{k}=2 ; \mathrm{x}=\cos \frac{4 \pi}{3}+\mathrm{i} \sin \frac{4 \pi}{3}
\end{aligned}$$
2. Find all the values of $(i)^{2 / 3}$.

\section*{Solution:}

Let $x=(i)^{2 / 3}$
$$\begin{aligned}
& =\left[\left(\cos \frac{\pi}{2}+i \sin \frac{\pi}{2}\right)\right]^{2 / 3} \\
& =\left[\left(\cos \frac{\pi}{2}+i \sin \frac{\pi}{2}\right)^{2}\right]^{1 / 3} \\
& =\left[\cos 2\left(\frac{\pi}{2}\right)+i \sin 2\left(\frac{\pi}{2}\right)\right]^{1 / 3} \\
& =[\cos \pi+i \sin \pi]^{1 / 3}
\end{aligned}$$
$$\begin{aligned}
& =[\cos (2 \mathrm{k} \pi+\pi)+\mathrm{i} \sin (2 \mathrm{k} \pi+\pi)]^{1 / 3} \\
& =\cos \left(\frac{2 \mathrm{k} \pi+\pi}{3}\right)+\mathrm{i} \sin \left(\frac{2 \mathrm{k} \pi+\pi}{3}\right) \quad \text { where } \mathrm{k}=0,1,2
\end{aligned}$$
when $k=0 ; \quad x=\cos \frac{\pi}{3}+i \sin \frac{\pi}{3}$
$$\begin{array}{ll}
\mathrm{k}=1 ; & \mathrm{x}=\cos \frac{3 \pi}{3}+\mathrm{i} \sin \frac{3 \pi}{3} \\
\mathrm{k}=2 ; & \mathrm{x}=\cos \frac{5 \pi}{3}+\mathrm{i} \sin \frac{5 \pi}{3}
\end{array}$$
3. Solve: $x^{2}+16=0$

\section*{Solution:}

Given: $x^{2}+16=0$
$$\begin{aligned}
\mathrm{x}^{2} & =-16=16 \times-1 \\
\mathrm{x} & =(16)^{1 / 2}(-1)^{1 / 2} \\
& =4[\cos \pi+\mathrm{i} \sin \pi]^{1 / 2} \\
& =4[\cos (2 \mathrm{k} \pi+\pi)+\mathrm{i} \sin (2 \mathrm{k} \pi+\pi)]^{1 / 2} \\
& =4\left[\cos \left(\frac{2 \mathrm{k} \pi+\pi}{2}\right)+\mathrm{i} \sin \left(\frac{2 \mathrm{k} \pi+\pi}{2}\right)\right] \text { where } \mathrm{k}=0,1
\end{aligned}$$
when $\mathrm{k}=0 ; \quad \mathrm{x}=4\left[\cos \frac{\pi}{2}+\mathrm{i} \sin \frac{\pi}{2}\right]$
$$\mathrm{k}=1 ; \quad \mathrm{x}=4\left[\cos \frac{3 \pi}{2}+\mathrm{i} \sin \frac{3 \pi}{2}\right]$$
4. If $\omega$ is the cube roots of unity then prove that $\left(1-\omega+\omega^{2}\right)^{5}+\left(1+\omega-\omega^{2}\right)^{5}=32$.

\section*{Solution:}

If $\omega$ is the cube roots of unity then $\omega^{3}=1$ and $1+\omega+\omega^{2}=0$.
LHS : $\left(1-\omega+\omega^{2}\right)^{5}+\left(1+\omega-\omega^{2}\right)^{5}$
$$\begin{aligned}
& =\left(1+\omega^{2}-\omega\right)^{5}+\left(1+\omega-\omega^{2}\right)^{5} \\
& =(-\omega-\omega)^{5}+\left(-\omega^{2}-\omega^{2}\right)^{5} \\
& =(-2 \omega)^{5}+\left(-2 \omega^{2}\right)^{5} \\
& =(-2)^{5} \omega^{5}+(-2)^{5}\left(\omega^{2}\right)^{5} \\
& =-32 \omega^{2}-32 \omega \\
& =-32\left(\omega^{2}+\omega\right) \\
& =-32(-1)=32=\text { RHS }
\end{aligned}$$

\section*{PART - C}
1. Solve: $\mathrm{x}^{7}+1=0$

\section*{Solution:}

Given: $\mathrm{x}^{7}+1=0$
$$\begin{aligned}
\mathrm{x}^{7} & =-1 \\
\mathrm{x} & =(-1)^{1 / 7} \\
& =(\cos \pi+\mathrm{i} \sin \pi)^{1 / 7} \\
& =[\cos (2 \mathrm{k} \pi+\pi)+\mathrm{i} \sin (2 \mathrm{k} \pi+\pi)]^{1 / 7} \\
& =\cos \left(\frac{2 \mathrm{k} \pi+\pi}{7}\right)+\mathrm{i} \sin \left(\frac{2 \mathrm{k} \pi+\pi}{7}\right) \text { where } \mathrm{k}=0,1,2,3,4,5,6,
\end{aligned}$$
$\therefore$ The values are
$$\begin{aligned}
& \text { when } \begin{aligned}
& \mathrm{k}=0 ; \quad \mathrm{x}=\cos \frac{\pi}{7}+i \sin \frac{\pi}{7} \\
& \mathrm{k}=1 ; \quad \mathrm{x}=\cos \frac{3 \pi}{7}+i \sin \frac{3 \pi}{7} \\
& \mathrm{k}=2 ; \quad \mathrm{x}=\cos \frac{5 \pi}{7}+i \sin \frac{5 \pi}{7} \\
& \mathrm{k}=3 ; \quad \mathrm{x}=\cos \frac{7 \pi}{7}+i \sin \frac{7 \pi}{7} \\
& \mathrm{k}=4 ; \quad \mathrm{x}=\cos \frac{9 \pi}{7}+i \sin \frac{9 \pi}{7} \\
& \mathrm{k}=5 ; \quad \mathrm{x}=\cos \frac{11 \pi}{7}+i \sin \frac{11 \pi}{7} \\
& \mathrm{k}=6 ; \quad \mathrm{x}=\cos \frac{13 \pi}{7}+i \sin \frac{13 \pi}{7}
\end{aligned}
\end{aligned}$$

\section*{2. Solve: $\mathrm{x}^{6}-1=0$}

\section*{Solution:}

Given: $x^{6}-1=0$
$$\begin{aligned}
x^{6} & =1 \\
x & =(1)^{1 / 6} \\
& =(\cos 0+i \sin 0)^{1 / 6} \\
& =(\cos 2 \mathrm{k} \pi+i \sin 2 \mathrm{k} \pi)^{1 / 6} \\
& =\cos \frac{2 \mathrm{k} \pi}{6}+i \sin \frac{2 \mathrm{k} \pi}{6} \quad \text { where } \mathrm{k}=0,1,2,3,4,5
\end{aligned}$$
$\therefore$ The values are,
when $\mathrm{k}=0 ; \mathrm{x}=\cos 0+\mathrm{i} \sin 0$
$$\begin{aligned}
& \mathrm{k}=1 ; \quad \mathrm{x}=\cos \frac{2 \pi}{6}+i \sin \frac{2 \pi}{6} \\
& \mathrm{k}=2 ; \quad \mathrm{x}=\cos \frac{4 \pi}{6}+i \sin \frac{4 \pi}{6} \\
& \mathrm{k}=3 ; \quad \mathrm{x}=\cos \frac{6 \pi}{6}+i \sin \frac{6 \pi}{6} \\
& \mathrm{k}=4 ; \quad \mathrm{x}=\cos \frac{8 \pi}{6}+i \sin \frac{8 \pi}{6} \\
& \mathrm{k}=5 ; \quad \mathrm{x}=\cos \frac{10 \pi}{6}+i \sin \frac{10 \pi}{6}
\end{aligned}$$
3. Solve: $x^{8}+x^{5}+x^{3}+1=0$

\section*{Solution:}
$$\begin{aligned}
& \text { Given: } \mathrm{x}^{8}+\mathrm{x}^{5}+\mathrm{x}^{3}+1=0 \\
& \begin{array}{l}
\mathrm{x}^{5}\left(\mathrm{x}^{3}+1\right)+1\left(\mathrm{x}^{3}+1\right)=0 \\
\left(\mathrm{x}^{5}+1\right)\left(\mathrm{x}^{3}+1\right)=0 \\
\mathrm{x}^{5}+1=0 \quad ; \mathrm{x}^{3}+1=0
\end{array}
\end{aligned}$$

Case (i)
$$\begin{aligned}
\mathrm{x}^{5} & +1=0 \\
\mathrm{x} & =(-1)^{1 / 5} \\
& =(\cos \pi+\mathrm{i} \sin \pi)^{1 / 5} \\
& =[\cos (2 \mathrm{k} \pi+\pi)+\mathrm{i} \sin (2 \mathrm{k} \pi+\pi)]^{1 / 5} \\
& =\cos \left(\frac{2 \mathrm{k} \pi+\pi}{5}\right)+\mathrm{i} \sin \left(\frac{2 \mathrm{k} \pi+\pi}{5}\right) \quad \text { where } \mathrm{k}=0,1,2,3,4
\end{aligned}$$

The roots are,
$$\text { when } \begin{aligned}
\mathrm{k} & =0 ; \quad \mathrm{x}=\cos \frac{\pi}{5}+\mathrm{i} \sin \frac{\pi}{5} \\
\mathrm{k} & =1 ; \quad \mathrm{x}=\cos \frac{3 \pi}{5}+\mathrm{i} \sin \frac{3 \pi}{5} \\
\mathrm{k} & =2 ; \quad \mathrm{x}=\cos \frac{5 \pi}{5}+\mathrm{i} \sin \frac{5 \pi}{5} \\
\mathrm{k} & =3 ; \quad \mathrm{x}=\cos \frac{7 \pi}{5}+i \sin \frac{7 \pi}{5} \\
\mathrm{k} & =4 ; \quad \mathrm{x}=\cos \frac{9 \pi}{5}+i \sin \frac{9 \pi}{5}
\end{aligned}$$

Case (ii) :
$$\begin{aligned}
& \mathrm{x}^{3}+1=0 \\
& \mathrm{x}=(-1)^{1 / 3} \\
&=(\cos \pi+\mathrm{i} \sin \pi)^{1 / 3} \\
&=[\cos (2 \mathrm{k} \pi+\pi)+\mathrm{i} \sin (2 \mathrm{k} \pi+\pi)]^{1 / 3} \\
&=\cos \left(\frac{2 \mathrm{k} \pi+\pi}{3}\right)+i \sin \left(\frac{2 \mathrm{k} \pi+\pi}{3}\right) \quad \text { where } \mathrm{k}=0,1,2 \\
& \text { when } \mathrm{k}=0 ; \mathrm{x}=\cos \frac{\pi}{3}+\mathrm{i} \sin \frac{\pi}{3} \\
& \quad \mathrm{k}=1 ; \mathrm{x}=\cos \frac{3 \pi}{3}+\mathrm{i} \sin \frac{3 \pi}{3} \\
& \quad \mathrm{k}=2 ; \mathrm{x}=\cos \frac{5 \pi}{3}+\mathrm{i} \sin \frac{5 \pi}{3}
\end{aligned}$$
4. Find all the values of $\left(\frac{1}{2}+i \frac{\sqrt{3}}{2}\right)^{3 / 4}$ and also prove that the product of the four values is 1 .

\section*{Solution:}

Let $a+i b=\frac{1}{2}+i \frac{\sqrt{3}}{2}=r(\cos \theta+i \sin \theta)$
Here $\mathrm{a}=\frac{1}{2} \quad \& \quad \mathrm{~b}=\frac{\sqrt{3}}{2}$
Modulus:
$r=\sqrt{a^{2}+b^{2}}=\sqrt{\left(\frac{1}{2}\right)^{2}+\left(\frac{\sqrt{3}}{2}\right)^{2}}=\sqrt{\frac{1}{4}+\frac{3}{4}}=\sqrt{1}=1$
Arugument:
$\theta=\tan ^{-1}\left(\frac{b}{a}\right)=\tan ^{-1}\left[\frac{\sqrt{3} / 2}{1 / 2}\right]=\tan ^{-1}(\sqrt{3})=\frac{\pi}{3}$
$\therefore$ (1) becomes,
$$\begin{aligned}
& \frac{1}{2}+i \frac{\sqrt{3}}{2}=1\left(\cos \frac{\pi}{3}+i \sin \frac{\pi}{3}\right) \\
& \Rightarrow\left(\frac{1}{2}+i \frac{\sqrt{3}}{2}\right)^{3 / 4}=\left(\cos \frac{\pi}{3}+i \sin \frac{\pi}{3}\right)^{3 / 4}
\end{aligned}$$
$$\begin{aligned}
& =\left[\left(\cos \frac{\pi}{3}+i \sin \frac{\pi}{3}\right)^{3}\right]^{\frac{1}{4}} \\
& \quad=\left[\cos 3\left(\frac{\pi}{3}\right)+i \sin 3\left(\frac{\pi}{3}\right)\right]^{1 / 4} \\
& \quad=[\cos \pi+\mathrm{i} \sin \pi]^{1 / 4} \\
& \quad=[\cos (2 \mathrm{k} \pi+\pi)+\mathrm{i} \sin (2 \mathrm{k} \pi+\pi)]^{1 / 4} \\
& \quad=\cos \left(\frac{2 \mathrm{k} \pi+\pi}{4}\right)+\mathrm{i} \sin \left(\frac{2 \mathrm{k} \pi+\pi}{4}\right) \quad \text { where } \mathrm{k}=0,1,2,3
\end{aligned}$$
$\therefore$ The values are,
$$\text { when } \begin{aligned}
\mathrm{k} & =0 ; \mathrm{R}_{1}=\cos \frac{\pi}{4}+\mathrm{i} \sin \frac{\pi}{4} \\
\mathrm{k} & =1 ; \mathrm{R}_{2}=\cos \frac{3 \pi}{4}+\mathrm{i} \sin \frac{3 \pi}{4} \\
\mathrm{k} & =2 ; \mathrm{R}_{3}=\cos \frac{5 \pi}{4}+\mathrm{i} \sin \frac{5 \pi}{4} \\
\mathrm{k} & =3 ; \mathrm{R}_{4}=\cos \frac{7 \pi}{4}+\mathrm{i} \sin \frac{7 \pi}{4}
\end{aligned}$$

\section*{Product of the four values}
$$\begin{aligned}
& \mathrm{R}_{1} \times \mathrm{R}_{2} \times \mathrm{R}_{3} \times \mathrm{R} \\
& =\left(\cos \frac{\pi}{4}+\mathrm{i} \sin \frac{\pi}{4}\right)\left(\cos \frac{3 \pi}{4}+\mathrm{i} \sin \frac{3 \pi}{4}\right)\left(\cos \frac{5 \pi}{4}+\mathrm{i} \sin \frac{5 \pi}{4}\right)\left(\cos \frac{7 \pi}{4}+\mathrm{i} \sin \frac{7 \pi}{4}\right) \\
& =\cos \left(\frac{\pi}{4}+\frac{3 \pi}{4}+\frac{5 \pi}{4}+\frac{7 \pi}{4}\right)+i \sin \left(\frac{\pi}{4}+\frac{3 \pi}{4}+\frac{5 \pi}{4}+\frac{7 \pi}{4}\right) \\
& =\cos \frac{16 \pi}{4}+i \sin \frac{16 \pi}{4} \\
& =\cos 4 \pi+i \sin 4 \pi \\
& =1+i(0)=1
\end{aligned}$$

4. Find the modulus and argument of the following complex numbers
(i) $\frac{1+\sqrt{3} i}{1+i}$
(ii) $\frac{2-\mathrm{i}}{3+7 \mathrm{i}}$
(iii) $\frac{1+i \sqrt{3}}{1-i}$
(iv) $\frac{(1+\mathrm{i})(1+2 \mathrm{i})}{1+3 \mathrm{i}}$
(v) $\frac{i-3}{i-1}$
(vi) $\frac{1-\mathrm{i}}{1+\mathrm{i}}$
5. Prove that the following complex numbers form a square.
(i) $9+\mathrm{i}, 4+13 \mathrm{i},-8+8 \mathrm{i},-3-4 \mathrm{i}$
(ii) $2+\mathrm{i}, 4+3 \mathrm{i}, 2+5 \mathrm{i}, 3 \mathrm{i}$
(iii) $-1,3 \mathrm{i}, 3+2 \mathrm{i}, 2-\mathrm{i}$
(iv) $4+5 \mathrm{i}, 1+2 \mathrm{i}, 4-\mathrm{i}, 7+2 \mathrm{i}$
6. Show that the following complex numbers form a rectangle.
(i) $1+2 \mathrm{i},-2+5 \mathrm{i}, 7 \mathrm{i}, 3+4 \mathrm{i}$
(ii) $4+3 i, 12+9 i, 15+5 i, 7-i$
(iii) $1+\mathrm{i}, 3+5 \mathrm{i}, 4+4 \mathrm{i}, 2 \mathrm{i}$
(iv) $8+4 \mathrm{i}, 5+7 \mathrm{i},-1+\mathrm{i}, 2-2 \mathrm{i}$
7. Show that the following complex number form a rhombus.
(i) $8+5 i, 16+11 i, 10+3 i, 2-3 i$
(ii) $6+4 \mathrm{i}, 4+5 \mathrm{i}, 6+3 \mathrm{i}, 8+\mathrm{i}$
(iii) $1+\mathrm{i}, 2+\mathrm{i}, 2+2 \mathrm{i}, 1+2 \mathrm{i}$
8. Simplify the following using De Moivre's theorem
(i) $\frac{(\cos 2 \theta-\mathrm{i} \sin 2 \theta)^{4}(\cos 4 \theta+\mathrm{i} \sin 4 \theta)^{-5}}{(\cos 3 \theta+\mathrm{i} \sin 3 \theta)^{2}(\cos 5 \theta-\mathrm{i} \sin 5 \theta)^{-3}}$
(ii) $\frac{(\cos 2 \theta-\mathrm{i} \sin 2 \theta)^{3}(\cos 3 \theta+\mathrm{i} \sin 3 \theta)^{4}}{(\cos 3 \theta+\mathrm{i} \sin 3 \theta)^{2}(\cos 5 \theta-\mathrm{i} \sin 5 \theta)^{-3}}$
(iii) $\frac{(\cos 2 \theta-\mathrm{i} \sin 2 \theta)^{7}(\cos 3 \theta+\mathrm{i} \sin 3 \theta)^{-5}}{(\cos 4 \theta+\mathrm{i} \sin 4 \theta)^{2}(\cos 5 \theta-\mathrm{i} \sin 5 \theta)^{-6}}$
(iv) $\frac{(\cos 3 \theta+\mathrm{i} \sin 3 \theta)^{2}(\cos 4 \theta-\mathrm{i} \sin 4 \theta)^{3}}{(\cos \theta+\mathrm{i} \sin \theta)^{3}} \quad$ when $\theta=\frac{\pi}{9}$
(v) $\frac{(\cos x-i \sin x)^{3}(\cos 3 x+i \sin 3 x)^{5}}{(\cos 2 x-i \sin 2 x)^{5}(\cos 5 x+i \sin 5 x)^{7}} \quad$ when $x=\frac{2 \pi}{13}$
(vi) $\frac{(\cos 5 \theta-\mathrm{i} \sin 5 \theta)(\cos 2 \theta-\mathrm{i} \sin 2 \theta)^{-3}}{(\cos \theta+i \sin \theta)^{5}(\cos 3 \theta+\mathrm{i} \sin 3 \theta)^{-5}} \quad$ when $\theta=\frac{2 \pi}{11}$
9. Show that $\left[\frac{\cos \theta+\mathrm{i} \sin \theta}{\sin \theta-\mathrm{i} \cos \theta}\right]^{4}=1$
10. Show that $\left[\frac{1+\cos \theta+\mathrm{i} \sin \theta}{1+\cos \theta-\mathrm{i} \sin \theta}\right]^{3}=\cos 3 \theta+\mathrm{i} \sin 3 \theta$
11. Prove that $\left[\frac{1+\sin \frac{\pi}{8}+i \cos \frac{\pi}{8}}{1+\sin \frac{\pi}{8}-i \cos \frac{\pi}{8}}\right]^{8}=1$.
12. If $\mathrm{a}=\cos \alpha+\mathrm{i} \sin \alpha, \mathrm{b}=\cos \beta+\mathrm{i} \sin \beta$ and $\mathrm{c}=\cos \gamma+\mathrm{i} \sin \gamma$ find the value of $\frac{\mathrm{ab}}{\mathrm{c}}+\frac{\mathrm{c}}{\mathrm{ab}}$.
13. If $\mathrm{x}=\cos 3 \alpha+\mathrm{i} \sin 3 \alpha, \mathrm{y}=\cos 3 \beta+\mathrm{i} \sin 3 \beta$ prove that
(i) $\sqrt[3]{x y}+\frac{1}{\sqrt[3]{x y}}=2 \cos (\alpha+\beta)$
(ii) $\sqrt[3]{\mathrm{xy}}-\frac{1}{\sqrt[3]{\mathrm{xy}}}=2 i \sin (\alpha+\beta)$
14. If $x=\cos \alpha+i \sin \alpha, y=\cos \beta+i \sin \beta, z=\cos \gamma+i \sin \gamma$ and if $x+y+z=0$ show that
(i) $\cos 3 \alpha+\cos 3 \beta+\cos 3 \gamma=3 \cos (\alpha+\beta+\gamma)$
(ii) $\sin 3 \alpha+\sin 3 \beta+\sin 3 \gamma=3 \sin (\alpha+\beta+\gamma)$
15. If $x=\cos \alpha+i \sin \alpha, y=\cos \beta+i \sin \beta$ and $z=\cos \gamma+i \sin \gamma$ and if $x+y+z=0$ prove that $\frac{1}{x}+\frac{1}{y}+\frac{1}{z}=0$
16. If $\mathrm{x}+\frac{1}{\mathrm{x}}=2 \cos \theta$ and $\mathrm{y}+\frac{1}{\mathrm{y}}=2 \cos \phi$ show that $\frac{\mathrm{x}^{m}}{\mathrm{y}^{\mathrm{m}}}+\frac{\mathrm{y}^{\mathrm{n}}}{\mathrm{x}^{\mathrm{m}}}=2 \cos (\mathrm{~m} \theta-\mathrm{n} \phi)$.
17. If $x=\cos \alpha+i \sin \alpha, y=\cos \beta+i \sin \beta$ prove that $x^{m} y^{n}+\frac{1}{x^{m} y^{n}}=2 \cos (m \alpha+n \beta)$.
18. If ' $n$ ' is a positive integer, prove that $(1+i \sqrt{3})^{n}+(1-i \sqrt{3})^{n}=2^{n+1} \cos \frac{n \pi}{3}$.
19. If ' $n$ ' is a positive integer prove that $(\sqrt{3}+i)^{n}-(\sqrt{3}-i)^{n}=2^{n+1} \cos \frac{n \pi}{6}$.
20. Prove that $(1+\cos \theta+i \sin \theta)^{n}+(1+\cos \theta-i \sin \theta)^{n}=2^{n+1} \cos ^{n}\left(\frac{\theta}{2}\right) \cos \left(\frac{n \theta}{2}\right)$.
21. Solve: $\mathrm{x}^{4}+1=0$
22. Solve: $\mathrm{x}^{5}+1=0$
23. Solve: $\mathrm{x}^{6}+1=0$
24. Solve: $\mathrm{x}^{4}-1=0$
25. Solve: $\mathrm{x}^{5}-1=0$
26. Solve: $x^{7}-1=0$
27. Solve: $x^{5}+x^{3}+x^{2}+1=0$
28. Solve: $x^{8}-x^{5}+x^{3}-1=0$
29. Solve: $x^{7}+x^{4}+x^{3}+1=0$
30. Solve: $x^{7}-x^{4}+x^{3}-1=0$

\section*{Introduction:}

Trigonometry is one of the oldest branches of Mathematics. The word Trigonometry is derived from the Greek words 'Trigonon' and 'metron' means measurement of angles. In olden days Trigonometry was mainly used as a tool for studying astronomy. In earlier stages Trigonometry was mainly concerned with angles of a triangle. But now it has its applications in various branches of science such as surveying, engineering, navigations etc. For the study of higher mathematics, knowledge of Trigonometry is essential.

\section*{Trigonometrical ratios:}

There are six Trigonometrical ratios sine, cosine, tangent, cotangent, secant and cosecant shortly written as $\sin \theta, \cos \theta, \tan \theta, \cot \theta, \sec \theta$ and $\operatorname{cosec} \theta$.

The side opposite to angle $\theta$ (Theta) is called opposite side. The side opposite to $90^{\circ}$ is called Hypotenuse. The other side is adjacent side.
$$\begin{array}{l|c}
\sin \theta=\frac{\text { Opp }}{\text { Hyp }} & \operatorname{cosec} \theta=\frac{1}{\sin \theta}=\frac{\text { Hyp }}{\text { Opp }} \\
\cos \theta=\frac{\text { adj }}{\text { Hyp }} & \sec \theta=\frac{1}{\cos \theta}=\frac{\text { Hyp }}{\text { adj }} \\
\tan \theta=\frac{\text { Opp }}{\text { adj }} & \cot \theta=\frac{1}{\tan \theta}=\frac{\text { adj }}{\text { opp }}
\end{array}$$
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_07_b62b456faa89cfbc166dg-075.jpg?height=427&width=720&top_left_y=1209&top_left_x=1011)

Fig.3.11

\section*{Note:}
$$\tan \theta=\frac{\sin \theta}{\cos \theta} \text { and } \cot \theta=\frac{\cos \theta}{\sin \theta}$$

\section*{Fundamental trigonometrical identities}
1) $\sin ^{2} \theta+\cos ^{2} \theta=1$
2) $1+\tan ^{2} \theta=\sec ^{2} \theta$
3) $1+\cot ^{2} \theta=\operatorname{cosec}^{2} \theta$

Trigonometrical ratios of known angles
\begin{tabular}{c|ccccc}
$\theta$ & 0 & 30 & 45 & 60 & 90 \\
\hline $\sin \theta$ & 0 & $\frac{1}{2}$ & $\frac{1}{\sqrt{2}}$ & $\frac{\sqrt{3}}{2}$ & 1 \\
$\cos \theta$ & 1 & $\frac{\sqrt{3}}{2}$ & $\frac{1}{\sqrt{2}}$ & $\frac{1}{2}$ & 0 \\
$\tan \theta$ & 0 & $\frac{1}{\sqrt{3}}$ & 1 & $\sqrt{3}$ & $\infty$
\end{tabular}

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_07_b62b456faa89cfbc166dg-076.jpg?height=691&width=1084&top_left_y=677&top_left_x=434)
